8 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Associated Builders & Contrs. v. City of Lansing


No. 149622


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


499 Mich. 177; 880 N.W.2d 765; 2016 Mich. LEXIS 869


October 15, 2015, Argued 
May 17, 2016, Decided 
May 17, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Associated Builders & Contrs. v. City of Lansing, 305 Mich. App. 395, 853 N.W.2d 433, 2014 Mich. App. LEXIS 971 (2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Under the current Constitution, there was no room for doubt about the expanded scope of authority of Michigan's cities and villages, and the powers over municipal concerns, property and government were to be liberally construed; [2]-If a municipality had broad powers over local concerns, it certainly had the power to set terms for the contracts it entered into with third parties for its own municipal projects--including provisions relating to the wages paid to third-party employees; [3]-The municipality controlled its own money, and expressed its citizens' preference as to what those who work on public projects should be paid; [4]-A previous case had no continuing viability and its rule that municipalities could not enact ordinances governing wages paid to employees on municipal construction contracts was overruled.


OUTCOME: Judgment vacated but result affirmed.

 

9 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dunbar


No. 150371


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


499 Mich. 60; 879 N.W.2d 229; 2016 Mich. LEXIS 463


November 4, 2015, Argued 
March 29, 2016, Decided 
March 29, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Dunbar v. Michigan, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 5642 (U.S., Oct. 3, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Dunbar, 306 Mich. App. 562, 857 N.W.2d 280, 2014 Mich. App. LEXIS 1662 (2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress, because the stop of defendant was lawful due to a violation of MCL 257.225(2) as the officers' view of the truck's registration plate was obstructed by the attached towing ball; [2]-MCL 257.225(2) prohibits an obstruction of a vehicle's registration plate; [3]-MCL 257.225(2) is not rendered "ambiguous" or "vague" merely because the statute casts a net so wide that it could be construed to make ordinary car equipment illegal.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

10 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Mich. Coal. of State Emple. Unions v. State


No. 147758


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


498 Mich. 312; 870 N.W.2d 275; 2015 Mich. LEXIS 1773; 203 L.R.R.M. 3599


January 13, 2015, Argued 
July 29, 2015, Decided 
July 29, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: The Michigan Coalition of State Employee Unions and others brought an action in the Court of Claims against the state of Michigan and various state agencies and officers, alleging that portions of 2011 PA 264, which amended the State Employees' Retirement Act (SERA), MCL 38.1 et seq., were unconstitutional because the resulting changes to retirement benefits altered rates of compensation or conditions of employment, which are within the exclusive authority of the Civil Service Commission to regulate. The Court of Claims, Joyce A. Draganchuk, J., granted plaintiffs' motion for summary disposition. The Court of Appeals, OWENS, P.J., and GLEICHER and STEPHENS, JJ., affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded, holding that SERA retirement benefits were properly classified as both "rates of compensation" and "conditions of employment," neither of which was subject to legislative alteration. 302 Mich App 187; 838 N.W.2d 708 (2013). The Supreme Court granted plaintiffs' application for leave to appeal. 495 Mich. 921, 844 N.W.2d 119 (2014) [***1] .
Mich. Coalition of State Emple. Unions v. State, 302 Mich. App. 187, 838 N.W.2d 708, 2013 Mich. App. LEXIS 1404 (2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because 2011 PA 264, the amendment to the State Employees Retirement Act (SERA), MCL 38.1 et seq., did not affect "rates of compensation" by requiring changes to wages or salaries, it did not implicate the Civil Service Commission's constitutional authority over classified civil servants' "rates of compensation," Const. 1963, art 11, § 5; [2]-The phrase "rates of compensation" in Const. 1963, art. 11, § 5 included only salaries and wages, i.e., amounts paid out to employees in a paycheck; [3]-The Commission had no authority to prevent the Legislature from enacting 2011 PA 264 any more than it had authority to compel the enactment of the SERA itself because either act would constitute an unconstitutional exercise of legislative authority, Const. 1963, art 3, § 2; [4]-SERA was not a product the Commission could have created by exercising its proper constitutional authority.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and case remanded.

 

11 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Int'l Union v. Green


No. 147700


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


498 Mich. 282; 870 N.W.2d 867; 2015 Mich. LEXIS 1772; 203 L.R.R.M. 3634; 165 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P61,616


January 13, 2015, Argued 
July 29, 2015, Decided 
July 29, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: The International Union, United Automobile, Aerospace, and Agricultural Implement Workers of America and others brought an action in the Court of Appeals against Nino E. Green and other members of the Michigan Employment Relations Commission, the Governor, and the Attorney General, seeking a declaratory judgment that portions of 2012 PA 349--which amended the public employment relations act (PERA), MCL 423.201 et seq., to prohibit public employers from requiring their employees to join a union or pay union-related expenses--were unconstitutional with respect to employees in the classified state civil service. The Court of Appeals, SAAD, P.J., and DONOFRIO, J. (GLEICHER, J., dissenting), held that the challenged portions of 2012 PA 349 were constitutional. 302 Mich App 246; 839 N.W.2d 1 (2013). The Supreme Court granted plaintiffs' application for leave to appeal. 495 Mich. 921, 843 N.W.2d 742 (2014) [***1] .
Int'l Union, UAW, UAW Local 6000 v. Green, 302 Mich. App. 246, 839 N.W.2d 1, 2013 Mich. App. LEXIS 2252 (2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Michigan Civil Service Commission lacks the authority to tax or appropriate - to wit, the authority to compel civil service employees to make involuntary financial contributions to subsidize the Commission's exercise of its constitutional duties and responsibilities; [2]-Although public collective bargaining is a method by which the Commission may choose to exercise its constitutional duties, it may not require collection of agency shop fees to fund its administrative operations in pursuit of those duties; [3]-The Commission's rules must yield to the Michigan Constitution when there is no authority for it to impose such fees.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed, albeit on different grounds.

 

12 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lockridge


No. 149073


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


498 Mich. 358; 870 N.W.2d 502; 2015 Mich. LEXIS 1774


January 15, 2015, Argued 
July 29, 2015, Decided 
July 29, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Michigan v. Lockridge, 136 S. Ct. 590, 193 L. Ed. 2d 487, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 7709 (U.S., 2015)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Certificate of appealability denied Lockridge v. Campbell, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 128674 (W.D. Mich., Sept. 21, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: Rahim Omarkhan Lockridge was convicted of involuntary manslaughter for the death of his wife, MCL 750.321, following a jury trial in the Oakland Circuit Court. His minimum sentence range calculated under the sentencing guidelines, MCL 777.1 et seq., was 43 to 86 months. The court, Nanci J. Grant, J., concluded that there were factors not accounted for in scoring the guidelines, including a probation violation, killing his wife in front of their three children, leaving the children at home with their mother dead on the floor, and prior domestic violence. Citing these as substantial and compelling reasons to depart from the minimum sentence range, MCL 769.34(3), the court sentenced defendant to a term of 8 years (96 months) to 15 years (the statutory maximum sentence). Defendant appealed, challenging both the scoring of the guidelines and the trial court's decision to exceed the guidelines minimum sentence range. While his case was pending in the Court of Appeals, the United States Supreme Court decided Alleyne v United States, 570 U.S.    ; 133 S Ct 2151; 186 L. Ed. 2d 314 (2013), which extended the rule of Apprendi v New Jersey, 530 U.S. 466; 120 S. Ct. 2348; 147 L. Ed. 2d 435 (2000), and held that a fact that increases either end of a defendant's sentencing range must have been admitted by the defendant or found by the jury beyond a reasonable doubt. After allowing defendant [***2] to file a supplemental brief challenging the guidelines scoring on Alleyne grounds, the Court of Appeals, BECKERING, P.J., and O'CONNELL and SHAPIRO, JJ., affirmed defendant's sentence in three separate opinions and rejected the Alleyne challenge. Judge O'CONNELL stated in the lead opinion that the panel was bound by People v Herron, 303 Mich App 392; 845 N.W.2d 533 (2013), which had rejected the same argument based on Alleyne. Judge BECKERING stated in her concurring opinion that had she not been bound by Herron, she would have held that requiring judicial fact-finding to set the guidelines mandatory minimum sentence range violated Alleyne and that the guidelines should be made advisory to cure the constitutional problem. In his concurring opinion, Judge SHAPIRO stated that he would have held that Alleyne only bars requiring judicial fact-finding to set the bottom of the minimum sentence range under the guidelines, so only the bottom of the range needed to be made advisory to cure the constitutional flaw. 304 Mich App 278; 849 N.W.2d 388 (2014). Defendant sought leave to appeal, and the Supreme Court granted his application to address the constitutional question presented by defendant's Alleyne challenge. 496 Mich. 852, 847 N.W.2d 925 (2014) [***1] .
People v. Lockridge, 304 Mich. App. 278, 849 N.W.2d 388, 2014 Mich. App. LEXIS 287 (2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because Michigan's sentencing guidelines scheme allowed judges to find by a preponderance of the evidence facts that were then used to compel an increase in the mandatory minimum punishment a defendant received, it violated U.S. Const. amend. VI under Alleyne; the Michigan Sentencing Guidelines were thus rendered advisory only; [2]-Because defendant received an upward departure sentence that did not rely on the minimum sentencing range from the improperly scored guidelines, he could not show prejudice from any error in scoring the offense variables (OVs) in violation of Alleyne.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed in part; defendant's sentence affirmed.

 

13 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


AFT Mich. v. State


No. 148748


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


497 Mich. 197; 866 N.W.2d 782; 2015 Mich. LEXIS 821


October 9, 2014, Argued 
April 8, 2015, Decided 
April 8, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: AFT Michigan and numerous other labor organizations representing public school employees brought an action in the Court of Claims against the state of Michigan, the State Treasurer, the Public School Employees' Retirement System, and others, asserting various constitutional challenges to 2012 PA 300, which had amended the Public School Employees Retirement Act, MCL 38.1301 et seq. In particular, the act added MCL 38.1391a(5), which enables current public school employees to opt out of retiree healthcare and thereby avoid paying the 3% retiree healthcare contributions required by MCL 38.1343e, a statute enacted in 2010 and subsequently struck down by the Court of Appeals as violating the Takings Clauses, Contracts Clauses, and Due Process Clauses of the Michigan and United States Constitutions in AFT Mich v Michigan, 297 Mich App 597; 825 N.W.2d 595 (2012). It also added MCL 38.1391a(8), which provides a separate retirement allowance for public school employees who elect to pay the 3% contributions but subsequently fail to qualify for retiree healthcare benefits. Furthermore, the act altered the manner in which public school employees accrue pension benefits. It increased the amount that all current public school employees must contribute in order to continue accruing pension benefits at the existing rate. MCL 38.1343g(1)(a) requires members of the retirement system's Basic Plan (who had not previously contributed to their pensions) [***2] to contribute 4% of their salaries to the retirement system for that purpose. MCL 38.1343g(1)(b) requires members of the retirement system's Member Investment Plan to contribute 7% of their salaries to the system. Employees who decline to make the additional contributions will accrue future pension benefits at a lower amount. Finally, MCL 38.1384b(3) and (4) allow employees to discontinue accruing future pension benefits altogether and participate in a 401(k)-style Tier 2 retirement account. The act, however, altered only the manner in which employees accrue pension benefits arising after the act's effective date. It had no effect on pension benefits previously accrued. Plaintiffs argued that the act violated the Takings Clauses, Const 1963, art 10, § 2 and US Const, Ams V and XIV; that the act impaired the obligation of contracts in violation of the Contracts Clauses, Const 1963, art 1, § 10 and US Const, art I, § 10, cl 1; and that the act violated the Due Process Clauses, Const 1963, art 1, § 17 and US Const, Am XIV, § 1. The Court of Claims, Rosemarie E. Aquilina, J., ruled in favor of defendants on all claims, and plaintiffs appealed. The Court of Appeals, SAAD, P.J., and K. F. KELLY, J. (GLEICHER, J., concurring), affirmed, concluding that contributions to the retiree healthcare program would be made voluntarily and were therefore free of constitutional infirmity and that the act did not affect [***3] any obligation of contracts between the state and public school employees with regard to the pension modification because the state is not obligated to provide future pension benefits to public school employees. 303 Mich App 651; 846 N.W.2d 583 (2014). Plaintiffs sought leave to appeal, which the Supreme Court granted. 495 Mich. 1002, 846 N.W.2d 544 (2014) [***1] .
AFT Michigan v. State, 303 Mich. App. 651, 846 N.W.2d 583, 2014 Mich. App. LEXIS 69 (2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-2012 PA 300, which amended the Michigan Public School Employees Retirement Act, MCL 38.1301 et seq., was upheld against constitutional challenges because it was not an unconstitutional taking as the retiree healthcare contributions under MCL 38.1343e were completely voluntary due to the opt out provision in MCL 38.1391a(5); [2]-2012 PA 300 did not violate the Contracts Clause of Const. 1963, art 1, § 10 and U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1, because no contracts existed between the state and public school employees creating rights to future pension benefits; [3]-2012 PA 300 did not violate "substantive" due process guarantees, because it served a legitimate governmental purpose to help fund retiree healthcare benefits while ensuring the continued financial stability of public schools.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

People v. Jones


No. 147735


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


497 Mich. 155; 860 N.W.2d 112; 2014 Mich. LEXIS 2402


October 9, 2014, Argued 
December 23, 2014, Decided 
December 23, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Jones, 302 Mich. App. 434, 839 N.W.2d 51, 2013 Mich. App. LEXIS 1493 (2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The circuit court erred by granting defendant's request that the jury be instructed on the misdemeanor lesser offense of moving violation causing death; [2]-The legislature specifically created an exception prohibiting an instruction on moving violation causing death where the charged offense is reckless driving causing death, and the legislature did not exceed its constitutional authority in doing so; [3]-As a result of defendant's charge of reckless driving causing death, MCL 257.626(5) barred an instruction on the misdemeanor lesser offense of moving violation causing death.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Adair v. State


No. 147794


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


497 Mich. 89; 860 N.W.2d 93; 2014 Mich. LEXIS 2401


October 9, 2014, Argued 
December 22, 2014, Decided 
December 22, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Adair v. State, 302 Mich. App. 305, 839 N.W.2d 680, 2013 Mich. App. LEXIS 1448 (2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A claim by plaintiffs taxpayers and school districts alleging legislative funding for new recordkeeping requirements was materially deficient, contrary to Mich. Const. art. 9, § 29, failed because a plaintiff alleging inadequate funding had to show the type and extent of the funding shortfall, but plaintiffs declined to provide such evidence; [2]-The school districts' acceptance of a payment under MCL 388.1622b(3)(c) did not waive their constitutional challenge because the statute contained no such language and only required the districts to comply with the state's reporting mandates.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; judgment for defendants.

 

16 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Hannay v. DOT


No. 146763, No. 147335


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


497 Mich. 45; 860 N.W.2d 67; 2014 Mich. LEXIS 2398


October 8, 2014, Argued 
December 19, 2014, Decided 
December 19, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Hunter v. Sisco, 300 Mich. App. 229, 832 N.W.2d 753, 2013 Mich. App. LEXIS 602 (2013)
Hannay v. DOT, 299 Mich. App. 261, 829 N.W.2d 883, 2013 Mich. App. LEXIS 85 (2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a tort suit arising from an accident involving plaintiff's vehicle and a DOT truck, the court held that a plaintiff may bring a third-party tort action for both economic damages, such as work-loss damages, and noneconomic damages, such as pain and suffering or emotional distress damages, against a governmental entity if the requirements under MCL 500.3135 have been met; [2]-Work-loss benefits that exceed the statutory maximum are available against a governmental entity; [3]-The trial court erred by purporting to award plaintiff work-loss damages under the no-fault act, MCL 500.3107, because she did not establish by a preponderance of the evidence that but for the accident, she would have earned wages as a dental hygienist; [4]-As plaintiff had not yet been accepted into the dental hygienist program, the connection between the accident and any loss income was too tenuous.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part; reversed and remanded in part.

 

17 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


IBM v. Dep't of Treasury


No. 146440


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


496 Mich. 642; 852 N.W.2d 865; 2014 Mich. LEXIS 1282


January 15, 2014, Argued 
July 14, 2014, Decided 
July 14, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by, Rehearing denied by, Stay denied by IBM v. Dep't of Treasury, 497 Mich. 894, 855 N.W.2d 512, 2014 Mich. LEXIS 2132 (2014)
Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by IBM v. Dep't of Treasury, 2016 Mich. App. LEXIS 1394 (Mich. Ct. App., July 21, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
International Business Machines Corporation (IBM) brought an action in the Court of Claims against the Department of Treasury, challenging the department's ruling that IBM was not entitled to apportion its business income tax base and modified gross receipts tax base using the three-factor apportionment formula provided in the Multistate Tax Compact, MCL 205.581 et seq., and was instead required to apportion its income using the sales-factor formula in the Business Tax Act, MCL 208.1101 et seq., when calculating its state taxes for 2008. Under this ruling, IBM was entitled to a refund of only $1,253,609 for the 2008 tax year rather than the $5,955,218 it had sought. IBM moved for summary disposition under MCR 2.116(C)(10), and the department moved for summary disposition under MCR 2.116(I)(2). After a hearing, the Court of Claims, Joyce A. Draganchuk, J., denied IBM's motion and granted summary disposition in favor of the department, ruling that the BTA mandated the use of the sales-factor apportionment formula. The Court of Appeals, Ronayne Krause, P.J., and Borrello, J. (Riordan, J., concurring), affirmed the Court of Claims order in an unpublished opinion per curiam issued November  [***2] 20, 2012 (Docket No. 306618). It held that because there was a facial conflict between the BTA's mandatory sales-factor apportionment formula and the Compact's elective three- factor apportionment formula, the Legislature had repealed the Compact's election provision by implication when it enacted the BTA. The Supreme Court granted IBM's application for leave to appeal. 494 Mich. 874, 832 N.W.2d 388 (2013). In a lead opinion by Justice Viviano, joined by Justices Cavanagh and Markman, and a concurring opinion by Justice Zahra, the Supreme Court held: The modified gross receipts tax is an income tax for purposes of the Multistate Tax Compact. IBM was entitled to use the Compact's elective three-factor apportionment formula to calculate its 2008 Michigan taxes. Court of Appeals judgment reversed; Court of Claims order granting summary disposition in favor of the Department reversed; case remanded to the Court of Claims for entry of an order granting summary disposition in favor of IBM. Justice Viviano, joined by Justices Cavanagh and Markman, held that the modified gross receipts tax fit within the Compact's broad definition of "income tax" by taxing a variation of net income, specifically, the entire  [***3] amount received by the taxpayer as determined from any gainful activity minus inventory and certain other deductions that are expenses not specifically and directly related to a particular transaction. He further concluded that the Court of Appeals erred by holding that the BTA had repealed the Compact's election provision by implication because the statutes could be reconciled when read in pari materia. Justice Zahra, concurring, agreed that IBM was entitled to use the Compact's elective apportionment formula for its 2008 Michigan taxes, and also that the tax bases at issue were "income taxes" within the meaning of the Compact. He would not have reached the question whether the Legislature repealed the Compact's election provision by implication when it enacted the BTA because the Legislature made clear that taxpayers were entitled to use the Compact's election provision for the 2008, 2009, and 2010 tax years. Justice McCormack, joined by Chief Justice Young and Justice Kelly, dissenting, would have affirmed the Court of Appeals judgment, concluding that allowing taxpayers to apportion their multistate income in accordance with the Compact's formula violated the Legislature's unambiguous  [***4] directive that taxes established under the BTA must be in accordance with the BTA's sales-only apportionment formula. She further concluded that there was no constitutional barrier that prevented the Legislature from making the Compact's alternative election provision unavailable to taxpayers.
IBM v. Dep't of Treasury, 2012 Mich. App. LEXIS 2293 (Mich. Ct. App., Nov. 20, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A corporation was entitled to use the Multistate Tax Compact's three-factor apportionment formula for its 2008 Michigan taxes; [2]-The express repeal of the Compact's election provision effective January 1, 2011, was evidence that the legislature had not impliedly repealed the provision when it enacted the Michigan Business Tax Act (BTA); [3]-The Compact's election provision remained in effect for the 2008 tax year; [4]-The corporation could use the Compact's apportionment formula for that portion of its tax base subject to the modified gross receipts tax for the 2008 tax year; [5]-The court of appeals erred by holding that the BTA repealed the Compact's election provision by implication.


OUTCOME: The Supreme Court reversed the court of appeals judgment in favor of the Department of Treasury, reversed the court of claims order granting summary disposition in favor of the Department, and remanded to the court of claims for entry of an order granting summary disposition in favor of the corporation.

Mick v. Kent County Sheriff's Dep't (In re Estate of Bradley)


No. 145055

July 26, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Estate of Bradley v. Kent County Sheriff's Dep't, 296 Mich. App. 31, 815 N.W.2d 799, 2012 Mich. App. LEXIS 560 (2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a civil contempt petition under MCL 600.1701 seeking indemnification damages under MCL 600.1721 from a county for negligent failure to execute a probate court's order to take a person into protective custody, which failure was alleged to have proximately caused the person's suicide, imposed tort liability within the meaning of MCL 691.1407(1). HOLDINGS: [1]-Tort liability encompassed all legal responsibility arising from noncontractual civil wrongs for which a remedy could be obtained in the form of compensatory damages; [2]-The indemnification remedy for civil contempt contemplated damages for a noncontractual civil wrong, thus falling squarely within the meaning of tort liability; [3]-Because the county was a governmental agency immunized from tort liability, its contemptuous conduct could not be punished through an award of indemnification damages.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

Hillsdale County Senior Servs. v. County of Hillsdale


No. 144630


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


494 Mich. 46; 832 N.W.2d 728; 2013 Mich. LEXIS 886


March 13, 2013, Argued 
May 31, 2013, Decided 
May 31, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Hillsdale County Senior Servs. Ctr. v. County of Hillsdale, 2012 Mich. App. LEXIS 1 (Mich. Ct. App., Jan. 3, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff senior service provider and others sought an order requiring defendant county to instruct all pertinent tax billing authorities or agencies to levy the full 0.5 mill required by an approved property-tax ballot proposition to fund services for older persons. The Court of Appeals of Michigan reversed the circuit court's judgment for mandamus for lack of subject-matter jurisdiction. The provider and others appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether the Michigan Tax Tribunal or the circuit court possessed jurisdiction over the mandamus claim. The instant appeal was a proceeding for direct review of the county board's final decision not to levy and spend the full 0.5 mill. The board was an agency under MCL 205.703(a), and the issue was one arising under the property tax laws pursuant to MCL 400.576 and Const. 1963, art 9, § 6. Thus, the central issue was whether the board's decision related to the issues set forth in MCL 205.731(a). The dispute did involve rates for the simple fact that both all or nothing, as argued by the provider, constituted a rate of taxation. The provider simply argued that the county was required to levy and spend more. Since all four elements of MCL 205.731(a) were satisfied, the tax tribunal possessed exclusive and original jurisdiction over the case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Debano-Griffin v. Lake County Bd. of Comm'rs


No. 143841


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


493 Mich. 167; 828 N.W.2d 634; 2013 Mich. LEXIS 146; 34 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1601; 163 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P61,324


October 10, 2012, Argued 
February 8, 2013, Decided 
February 8, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Debano-Griffin v. Lake County & Lake County Bd. of Comm'Rs, 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 1536 (Mich. Ct. App., Aug. 25, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Court of Appeals reversed the trial court's judgment that denied defendant employer's summary disposition motion that was filed after plaintiff terminated employee filed a claim under the Whistleblowers' Protection Act, MCL 15.361 et seq., against it for terminating her employment following her reporting of alleged misusage of millage funds. The state supreme court granted her application for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: The terminated employee filed a whistleblower claim under MCL 15.362, asserting that she was terminated for her complaints regarding a transfer of funds and use of an ambulance service that appeared to violate a millage the county voters had passed. The employer moved for summary disposition under MCR 2.116(C)(8) and (10), and claimed she had not made a prima facie case under the Whistleblower Protection Act (WPA). The trial court denied the motion. A jury returned a verdict in her favor. OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the appellate court's judgment and reinstated the trial court's denial of the employer's motion for summary disposition.

Mattison v. Soc. Sec. Comm'r (In re Certified Question)


No. 144385


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


493 Mich. 70; 825 N.W.2d 566; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 2222; Unemployment Ins. Rep. (CCH) P15,101C


November 15, 2012, Argued 
December 21, 2012, Decided 
December 21, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Mattison v. Comm'r of Soc. Sec. (In re Certified Question), 822 N.W.2d 221, 2012 Mich. LEXIS 1901 (Mich., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant commissioner denied plaintiff applicant's request to receive social security survivors' benefits for twins who were not conceived until 12 days after the father died, through the use of artificial insemination, the federal trial court certified the question to the state supreme court the threshold question for receiving such benefits about whether the twins could inherit from the father under Michigan's intestacy laws.


OVERVIEW: The mother gave birth to twins who were conceived by artificial insemination after their father had died. The mother sought social security survivors' benefits for the children based on the father's earnings. The commissioner denied her application, and an administrative law judge affirmed that decision. The mother then filed an action in federal trial court challenging the decision. That court asked the state supreme court to rule on the determinative issue, which was whether the children could inherit from the father under Michigan intestacy law. Only if they could inherit would they be entitled to social security survivors' benefits. The state supreme court then found that under Michigan intestacy law, the mother's children could not inherit from the father. It determined that because the mother's twins were not in gestation at the time of the father's death, given that the mother's eggs were not inseminated with his sperm until 12 days after he died, no inheritance rights vested in them at that time pursuant to MCL 700.2108 and because the twins were not living at the time of his death, they had no inheritance rights as heirs pursuant to MCL 700.2104.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court answered the certified question in the negative.

Atkins v. Suburban Mobility Auth. for Reg'l Transp.


No. 140401


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


492 Mich. 707; 822 N.W.2d 522; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 1253


March 7, 2012, Argued 
August 20, 2012, Decided 
August 20, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Atkins v. Suburban Mobility Auth., 821 N.W.2d 568; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 1841 (Mich., Oct. 24, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Vivian Atkins v. Suburban Mobility Auth. for Reg'l Transp., 2009 Mich. App. LEXIS 2250 (Mich. Ct. App., Oct. 22, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant transportation authority challenged a decision from the Michigan Court of Appeals, which reversed a trial court's decision to grant it partial summary disposition in a personal injury case filed by appellee bus passenger.


OVERVIEW: The passenger was injured in a bus accident. She filed an application for no-fault benefits with the transportation authority's insurer soon after, but she waited more than 7 months to notify the transportation authority that she might pursue liability in tort. Partial summary disposition was entered for the transportation service, but the court of appeals reversed. This appeal followed. In reversing the court of appeals, the supreme court determined that MCL 124.419 required that notice be given to the transportation authority within 60 days. The application for no-fault insurance benefits, even when supplemented with presumed "institutional knowledge" of the underlying facts of the injury, did not constitute written notice of a third-party tort claim against the transportation authority sufficient to comply with MCL 124.419. The provisions of MCL 124.419 applied to "ordinary claims" that arose in connection with a common carrier, and the 60-day notice requirement pertained to such claims for personal injury or property damage. An ordinary claim against a common carrier did not include claims made for first-party no-fault benefits.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed, and the case was remanded for reentry of the trial court's order.


 

35 of 1632 DOCUMENTS



McCahan v. Brennan


No. 142765


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


492 Mich. 730; 822 N.W.2d 747; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 1257


March 6, 2012, Argued 
August 20, 2012, Decided 
August 20, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: McCahan v. Brennan, 291 Mich. App. 430, 804 N.W.2d 906, 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 210 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Court of Claims granted appellee university summary disposition, finding that appellant driver's failure to file notice of intent to file a claim or the claim itself within the six-month deadline required dismissal of her claim. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed. The driver petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The driver argued that her claim, being a claim for personal injury, was not subject to the dictates or bar-to-claims language of MCL 600.6431(1). She argued that only subsection (3) governed her claim and acted as an independent provision that excluded application of subsection (1). The supreme court found that a reasonable person reading the statute would understand that subsections (1) and (3) were related and interdependent. The context of the entire statutory provision indicated that the six-month filing requirement for personal injury or property damage cases was a modification of the generally applicable one-year filing requirement. The failure to file a compliant claim or notice of intent to file a claim against the state within the relevant time periods designated in either subsection (1) or (3) would trigger the statute's prohibition. Because the driver failed to file any notice of an intent to pursue a claim against the university with the Court of Claims within six months, the driver's claim was barred.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.


 

36 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Likine


No. 141154, No. 141181, No. 141513


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


492 Mich. 367; 823 N.W.2d 50; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 1228



July 31, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Harris, 793 N.W.2d 435, 2011 Mich. LEXIS 74 (Mich., 2011)
People of the Mich. v. Michael Joseph Parks, 2010 Mich. App. LEXIS 702 (Mich. Ct. App., Apr. 20, 2010)
People v. Likine, 288 Mich. App. 648, 794 N.W.2d 85, 2010 Mich. App. LEXIS 1037 (2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In prosecutions under MCL 750.165 for failure to pay court-ordered child support, one defendant was convicted by a jury, a second by the court, a third pled guilty. The Court of Appeals, Michigan, affirmed the convictions in the first two cases and in the third denied a delayed application for leave to appeal. The court granted further review to consider the constitutionality of the rule that inability to pay was not a defense.


OVERVIEW: The Michigan Supreme Court agreed with the holding that MCL 750.165 imposes strict liability because it does not require a mens rea, and that evidence of a defendant's inability to pay, without more, is not a valid defense to a charge of felony nonsupport. The court also held, however, that a defendant charged with felony nonsupport may, in exceptional circumstances, on making the requisite evidentiary showing, establish impossibility as a defense. To establish that defense, a defendant must show that he or she acted in good faith and made all reasonable efforts to comply with the family court order, but could not do so through no fault of his or her own. Sufficient bona fide efforts to seek employment or borrow money in order to pay certainly are expected, but standing alone will not necessarily establish an impossibility defense. Instead, defendants must make all reasonable efforts, use all resources at their disposal to comply with support obligations, and establish that it is not reasonably possible to obtain the resources to pay. One of the defendant's in the current appeal should have been permitted to submit evidence of her mental illness, incapacitation, and disability.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction of the first defendant and remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings. The court affirmed the judgment of the Court of Appeals in the second case. The third defendant, who entered an unconditional guilty plea, affirmatively waived the impossibility defense and was therefore not entitled to relief.

 

37 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rapp


No.143343; 143344


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


492 Mich. 67; 821 N.W.2d 452; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 1220



July 27, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Rapp, 293 Mich. App. 159, 809 N.W.2d 665, 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 1093 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with the misdemeanor offense of violating Michigan State University Ordinance § 15.05. A district court jury convicted defendant of violating the ordinance. On appeal, the circuit court reversed the conviction on the basis that the ordinance was unconstitutionally overbroad on its face. The Michigan Court of Appeals reversed the circuit court's conclusion that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance made it a crime to disrupt the normal activity of any person, firm, or agency carrying out service, activity or agreement for or with the university. The ordinance prohibited disruptions but did not specify the types of disruptions that were prohibited. Thus, the plain language of the ordinance allowed its enforcement for even verbal disruptions. The verbal disruptions that the ordinance criminalized were not limited to those containing fighting words or obscene language. Also, the ordinance protected a broad class of individuals. Thus, the ordinance criminalized a substantial amount of constitutionally protected speech under the First Amendment. Therefore, the language in the ordinance making it an offense to disrupt the normal activity of a protected person was facially unconstitutional. Because the case was a criminal matter, MCR 7.101(O) did not provide grounds for awarding costs. Thus, there was no basis to undermine the broad statutory discretion granted the prosecution in its charging decisions, and the assessment of costs against the prosecution was improper.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the portion of the court of appeals' judgment pertaining to the constitutionality of the ordinance and reinstated the circuit court's decision that the ordinance was facially unconstitutional. The supreme court affirmed the portion of the court of appeals' judgment that costs could not be assessed against the prosecution under MCR 7.101(O) in criminal matters.

Mich. Props., L.L.C. v. Meridian Twp.


Nos. 143085, 143086, and 143087, No. 143281


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


491 Mich. 518; 817 N.W.2d 548; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 796



June 14, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Mich. Props., L.L.C. v. Meridian Twp., 492 Mich. 859; 817 N.W.2d 110, 2012 Mich. LEXIS 1219 (Mich., 2012)
On remand at, Remanded by Toll Northville Ltd. P'ship v. Twp. of Northville, LLC, 2012 Mich. App. LEXIS 1864 (Mich. Ct. App., Sept. 25, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: MJC/Lotus Group v. Brownstown Twp., 293 Mich. App. 1, 809 N.W.2d 605, 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 1001 (2011)
Mich. Props., LLC v. Meridian Twp., 292 Mich. App. 147, 808 N.W.2d 506, 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 607 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Court of Appeals granted judgment in favor of appellee taxpayer by reversing the Tax Tribunal and finding that the March board of review could not uncap the taxable values relating to the December 2004 transfers. Respondent city petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the issue before it was whether the failure of a taxing authority's assessor to adjust the taxable value of real property in the year immediately following a transfer of the property, in accordance with MCL 211.27a(3), precluded a March board of review from adjusting the taxable value in a subsequent year. The city's assessor failed to update the tax rolls to reflect the uncapped taxable values of the taxpayer's recently purchased properties for tax year 2005. The taxable value as entered on the tax rolls for 2005 violated the automatic requirement in MCL 211.27a(3) that the taxable value for the year following the transfer was the uncapped value. The supreme court reinstated the Tax Tribunal's decision affirming the March board of review's correction of the tax rolls to reflect the properly adjusted taxable values. In the second case, the Tax Tribunal had the ability to prospectively adjust the timely challenged taxable values of the taxpayer's parcels for tax year 2001 and subsequent years because the tax year 200 taxable value of the parent parcel was erroneous as a result of the inclusion of unconstitutional additions.


OUTCOME: The judgments were reversed and remanded to the court of appeals.

 

39 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Watkins


No. 142031, No. 142751


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


491 Mich. 450; 818 N.W.2d 296; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 786



June 8, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Watkins, 492 Mich. 859, 817 N.W.2d 111, 2012 Mich. LEXIS 1218 (2012)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Summary judgment denied by, Motion denied by, As moot, Certificate of appealability denied Watkins v. Woods, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 27631 ( E.D. Mich., Feb. 28, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Watkins, 2010 Mich. App. LEXIS 1871 (Mich. Ct. App., Oct. 5, 2010)
People v. Pullen, 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 300 (Mich. Ct. App., Feb. 15, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In one case, the Michigan Court of Appeals concluded that the trial court abused its discretion when it failed to determine which aspects of the victim's testimony met the requirements of MCL 768.27a and, in another, found that MRE 403 applied to evidence admissible under MCL 768.27a and the trial court did not abuse its discretion by excluding the evidence. Defendant and the State petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that because a defendant's propensity to commit a crime made it more probable that he committed the charged offense, MCL 768.27a permitted the admission of evidence that MRE 404(b) precluded. The statute established an exception to MRE 404(b) in cases involving a charge of sexual misconduct against a minor. The reasons for enacting the statute were to address a substantive concern about the protection of children and the prosecution of persons who perpetuated certain enumerated crimes against children and were more likely than others to reoffend. MCL 768.27a superseded MRE 404(b). The statute could be read in harmony with MRE 403, and the supreme court had to give effect to the permissive term "may" and the phrase "notwithstanding MCL 768.27" that were present in MCL 768.27a but absent from MCL 768.27b. MRE 403 applied to evidence admissible under MCL 768.27a. Courts had to weigh the propensity evidence in favor of the evidence's probative value. In the first case, any error in admitting all of the victim's testimony was harmless. The trial court erred by misapplying MRE 403 in the second case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in the first case; the judgments of the lower courts were vacated in the other case and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

41 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Moreno


No. 141837


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


491 Mich. 38; 814 N.W.2d 624; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 463



April 20, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People of the Mich. v. Angel Moreno, 2010 Mich. App. LEXIS 1055 (Mich. Ct. App., June 10, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Michigan Court of Appeals, which affirmed a trial court's decision to deny a motion to quash the charges filed against defendant under MCL 750.81d(1) and MCL 750.81d(2).


OVERVIEW: A struggle ensued after officers entered a home unlawfully, and defendant was charged with resisting and obstructing a police officer under MCL 750.81d. The trial court denied a request to quash, and the court of appeals affirmed. This appeal followed. In reversing the court of appeals, the supreme court determined that MCL 750.81d did not abrogate the common-law right to resist illegal police conduct, including unlawful arrests and unlawful entries into constitutionally protected areas. While the Michigan Legislature had the authority to modify the common law, it had to do so by speaking in no uncertain terms. Neither the language of MCL 750.81d nor the legislative history of this statute indicated with certainty that the Legislature intended to abrogate the common-law right to resist unlawful arrests or other unlawful invasions of private rights. The supreme court was unable to presume that the Legislature intended to abrogate this right. Therefore, it overruled People v Ventura, 262 Mich. App. 370; 686 N.W.2d 748 (2004) to the extent that it held that the Legislature affirmatively chose to modify the traditional common-law rule that a person may resist an unlawful arrest.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court with directions.

Houston v. Governor


SC: 144691, SC: 144768


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


491 Mich. 876; 810 N.W.2d 255; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 322



March 26, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
COA: 308724. Ingham CC: 12-000010-CZ.

COA: 308725. Ingham CC: 12-000010-CZ.
Houston v. Governor, 295 Mich. App. 588; 2012 Mich. App. LEXIS 403 (Mich. Ct. App., Mar. 7, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court heard oral arguments on applications for leave to appeal a judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that 2011 PA 280, which amended the County Apportionment Act, was not a "local" act in violation of Const. 1963, art 4, § 29. 2011 PA 280 nullified Oakland County's 25-commissioner district plan and established in its place a 21-commissioner district plan. 2011 PA 280 included a transitional provision requiring any county not in compliance with the Act to reapportion within 30 days of the law's effective date. As a practical matter, because Oakland County was the only county that would not be in compliance with the law on its effective date, only Oakland County would have to reapportion during the transitional period. That Oakland County may have been the only county immediately out of compliance with the Act, and therefore immediately affected by the transitional provision, did not frustrate the Act's general applicability. The appellate court erred by finding that the transitional provision in 2011 PA 280 constituted a "local act."


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was reversed.

In re Justin


No. 142076


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


490 Mich. 394; 809 N.W.2d 126; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 43



January 27, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by In re Justin, 491 Mich. 870; 809 N.W.2d 563; 2012 Mich. LEXIS 283 (Mich., Mar. 21, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Justin, 804 N.W.2d 111, 2011 Mich. LEXIS 1767 (Mich., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Judicial Tenure Commission (JTC) recommended the removal of respondent, a district court judge, from office for numerous instances of documented judicial misconduct by failing to follow the law, behaving as if he was above the law, and lying under oath during a proceeding before the JTC.


OVERVIEW: The judge was charged with misconduct for acts such as "fixing" traffic citations issued to himself, his spouse, and his staff; dismissing cases without conducting hearings or involving the prosecutor; failing to follow plea agreements; and making false statements under oath during the JTC hearing. On review, the court held that the duration, scope, and sheer number of respondent judge's substantiated acts of misconduct were without precedent in Michigan judicial disciplinary cases. The judge's long-term pattern of judicial misconduct constituted a negation of the proper exercise of judicial authority that more than justified his removal from office. His misconduct evinced an unacceptable disregard for the role of judge as well as disdain for due process and the right of parties to a fair hearing. His intentional abuses of judicial power to benefit himself, his spouse, and his staff were inconsistent with his oath of office and deleterious to the integrity and honor of the judiciary. His belief that he was above the law and not as subject to the law as those that appeared before him rendered him unworthy of holding judicial office.


OUTCOME: The judge was removed from his judicial office.

46 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dowdy


No. 140603


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


489 Mich. 373; 802 N.W.2d 239; 2011 Mich. LEXIS 1191



July 11, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Dowdy, 287 Mich. App. 278, 787 N.W.2d 131, 2010 Mich. App. LEXIS 214 (2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals that affirmed a circuit court order dismissing a charge of failing to register as a sex offender under MCL 28.729(1)(a) and (2)(a), arguing that the lower courts erred in concluding that homelessness precluded defendant from complying with the act.


OVERVIEW: In 1984, defendant was convicted of kidnapping and five counts of first-degree criminal sexual conduct. When he was released from prison in 2002, he was required to comply with the Sex Offenders Registration Act (SORA), MCL 28.721 et seq., which required him to register as a sex offender. In 2006, defendant was charged with violating the registration requirement. He moved to dismiss, arguing that SORA required him to register an address in order to comply with his statutory obligations but that, because he was homeless and did not have an address, he was unable to comply with the obligation to report a "residence" to police under SORA. The circuit court found his argument persuasive and dismissed. On review, the court held that homelessness was not a bar to compliance with SORA because homelessness did not preclude an offender from entering a police station and reporting to a law enforcement agency regarding the offender's residence or domicile. The legislature intended SORA to be a comprehensive system that required all sex offenders to register, whether homeless or otherwise.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for trial.

48 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Slaughter


No. 141009


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


489 Mich. 302; 803 N.W.2d 171; 2011 Mich. LEXIS 1175



July 1, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Slaughter, 2010 Mich. App. LEXIS 499 (Mich. Ct. App., Mar. 16, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals that affirmed a circuit court's suppression order, arguing that firefighters' warrantless entry into defendant's home fell within the community caretaking exception to the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV, requirement that a warrant be obtained before a residence could be entered.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's neighbor saw water running down her basement wall and over her electrical box; she heard water flowing behind that wall, which adjoined defendant's townhouse. Unable to locate defendant, the neighbor called 911. The city dispatched several firefighters, who entered defendant's residence. They went into the basement to turn off the water and assess whether additional measures had to be taken to prevent a fire; in plain view in the basement were grow lights and several dozen marijuana plants. The firefighters reported their findings to the police, who obtained a search warrant. Defendant moved to suppress, and the trial court granted the motion, finding that the firefighters violated defendant's Fourth Amendment rights. On review of the appellate court judgment affirming the trial court's suppression order, the court held that the community caretaking exception applied to firefighters no less than to police officers when they were responding to emergency situations that threatened life or property. Further, the firefighters' actions were reasonable under the circumstances, thus satisfying the community caretaking exception to the warrant requirement.


OUTCOME: The decision of the circuit court and the judgment of the court of appeals were reversed, and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

 

49 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Midland Cogeneration Venture L.P. v. Naftaly


No. 140814, No. 140817, No. 140818, No. 140819, No. 140820, No. 140821, No. 140822, No. 140823, No. 140824


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


489 Mich. 83; 803 N.W.2d 674; 2011 Mich. LEXIS 796



May 23, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Iron Mt. Info. Mgmt. v. Naftaly, 286 Mich. App. 616, 780 N.W.2d 923, 2009 Mich. App. LEXIS 2700 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs owned property that was subject to property taxes. The Michigan State Tax Commission denied the request to reclassify the property. Plaintiffs individually sought and obtained relief in various circuit courts. Defendants appealed the judgments in the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed each of the circuit court judgments and remanded for the entry of orders granting summary disposition in defendants' favor. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Michigan State Tax Commission (STC) property-classification decisions were final decisions of an administrative agency that were quasi-judicial in nature and affected private rights. Consequently, Const. 1963, art 6, § 28 guaranteed judicial review of them, and that guarantee could not be jettisoned by statute. Because MCL 211.34c(6) eliminated any appeal of a final administrative decision that was quasi-judicial in nature and affected private rights, it ran afoul of the guarantee of judicial review in Const. 1963, art 6, § 28. Because the final sentence of MCL 211.34c(6) precluded judicial review of STC classification decisions, the supreme court declared that sentence unconstitutional and severed it, leaving the remainder of MCL 211.34c(6) in full force and effect. Because judicial review had not otherwise been provided by law, MCL 600.631 applied and the circuit courts had subject matter jurisdiction over appeals of a decision of the STC regarding property classifications. Thus, plaintiffs could appeal the decisions in the cases before the supreme court in the circuit court for the county of which plaintiffs were residents or the circuit court for Ingham County, Michigan.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals.

 

50 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lown


No. 139969


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


488 Mich. 242; 794 N.W.2d 9; 2011 Mich. LEXIS 70



January 14, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Lown, 2009 Mich. App. LEXIS 2031 (Mich. Ct. App., Oct. 1, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals that concluded that the trial court properly denied his motion to dismiss a home-invasion charge because the statutory 180-day rule of MCL 780.131 and MCL 780.133 was satisfied.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court addressed the correct interpretation of the statutory "180-day rule" established by MCL 780.131 and MCL 780.133. The rule did not require that a trial be commenced or completed within 180 days of the date notice was delivered. Rather, it was sufficient that the prosecutor proceed promptly and moved the case to the point of readiness for trial within the 180-day period. The supreme court held that good faith was an implicit component of proper action by the prosecutor, who could not satisfy the rule simply by taking preliminary steps toward trial but then delaying inexcusably. The statutory 180-day period was a fixed period of consecutive days beginning on the date when the prosecutor received the required notice from the Michigan Department of Corrections (DOC). The 180-day rule was satisfied as to defendant because the prosecutor commenced action well within 180 days after receiving notice from DOC, and the record contained no evidence that ensuing delays caused in part by docket congestion were without reason or otherwise inexcusable under the facts of the case; indeed, defendant either requested or explicitly consented to each adjournment.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals was affirmed.

52 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Tennyson


No. 137755


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


487 Mich. 730; 790 N.W.2d 354; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1713



September 7, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Tennyson, 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 2054 (Mich. Ct. App., Oct. 16, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted leave to appeal to consider whether evidence that a child was present in a home in which defendant was in possession of drugs and firearms was, by itself, legally sufficient to support defendant's conviction under MCL 750.145 for doing an act that tended to cause a minor child to become neglected or delinquent so as to tend to come under the jurisdiction of the family division of the circuit court.


OVERVIEW: The essential elements derived from construing MCL 750.145 in pari materia with MCL 712A.2 required the court to find whether, taking the evidence in the light most favorable to the prosecutor, a rational trier of fact could have found that defendant's actions tended to cause the child to become delinquent or neglected, such that the child tended to come under family court jurisdiction. The court found that the prosecutor presented no evidence regarding the child's education, behavioral history, relationships with his peers, or any other relevant fact that could support the conclusion that defendant's actions tended to cause the child, to become delinquent. Moreover, the record did not establish that the criminality had any impact on the child's home. Therefore, in the instant case, where the jury was presented with no evidence other than that a child was present in a home where criminal activity occurred, the court held that a rational fact-finder could not have concluded beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant tended to cause the child to become delinquent or neglected so as to tend to come under family court jurisdiction, as those terms were defined by MCL 712A.2.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed in part, and defendant's conviction for doing an act that tended to cause a minor child to become neglected or delinquent so as to tend to come under the jurisdiction of the family division of the circuit court was vacated and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings. Defendant's convictions for drug and firearm possession remained intact.

People v. Jackson


No. 138988


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


487 Mich. 783; 790 N.W.2d 340; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1712



September 7, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Jackson, 2009 Mich. App. LEXIS 663 (Mich. Ct. App., Mar. 26, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which denied his request for a remand of his case for resentencing. Defendant was convicted of armed-robbery. The Court of Appeals vacated his concurrent convictions for felonious assault, which were used as a factor in calculating his sentence for armed robbery. Defendant filed an application for leave to appeal with the Court.


OVERVIEW: The Court found that defendant's sentence was based on inaccurate information, therefore, he was entitled to resentencing. As such, the Court of Appeals erred by failing to remand for resentencing. The Court held that remands for resentencing were governed by MCL 769.34, which required cases to be remanded when the sentence was based on inaccurate information. The Court held that defendant was entitled to resentencing because his sentence was based on inaccurate information due to the Court of Appeals vacating his felonious assault convictions. The Court further concluded that because defendant requested a remand for resentencing as part of his appeal to vacate the felonious-assault convictions, he complied with the requirement of MCL 769.34(10), which mandated that a request for remand be made in a proper motion filed in the Court of Appeals.


OUTCOME: The Court reversed that portion of the judgment in which the Court of Appeals concluded that it could not remand for resentencing. The Court, therefore, vacated defendant's sentence and remanded his case for resentencing.

Regents of the Univ. of Mich. v. Titan Ins. Co.


No. 136905


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


487 Mich. 289; 791 N.W.2d 897; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1658



July 31, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Univ. of Mich. Regents v. Titan Ins. Co., 2010 Mich. LEXIS 2102 (Mich., Oct. 15, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Univ. of Mich. Regents v. Titan Ins. Co., 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 1184 (Mich. Ct. App., June 5, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Appellate Court affirmed the trial court's judgment that found plaintiffs, a state university's health system and regents, were not entitled to recover payment from defendant insurance company, the servicing insurer for claims, for the cost of treating an uninsured man severely injured in an auto accident. The state supreme court granted leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: An uninsured man was severely injured in an auto accident. The state university's health system provided him with medical treatment. Less than one year after the accident, he sought personal protection insurance benefits. Because he was not covered under a no-fault insurance policy, the state's assigned claims facility designated the insurance company as the servicing insurer for his claims. Nearly five years after the accident, the state university's health system and regents sued the insurance company, and sought payment from it for his medical treatment. The trial court dismissed the lawsuit upon finding that the one-year back rule of MCL 500.3145(1) barred them recovering the claimed damages. The appellate court affirmed. The state supreme court found that even though the one-year-back rule in MCL 500.3145(1) would otherwise bar the recovery of damages, it had to be considered alongside MCL 600.5821(4) allowing claims by the entities listed therein, the State and its subdivisions without regard to limitations period. It then determined that its Cameron decision and the Liptow appellate ruling dictating a contrary result had to be overruled, as they had proved to be unworkable.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the appellate court's judgment and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Bezeau v. Palace Sports & Entm't, Inc.


No. 137500


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


487 Mich. 455; 795 N.W.2d 797; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1656



July 31, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Bezeau v. Palace Sports & Entm't, Inc., 488 Mich. 874; 788 N.W.2d 457; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1920 (Mich., Sept. 28, 2010)
Rehearing denied by Bezeau v. Palace Sports & Entm't, Inc., 488 Mich. 904; 793 N.W.2d 813; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 2118 (Mich., Oct. 22, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Bezeau v. Palace Sports & Entm't, Inc., 483 Mich. 1001, 764 N.W.2d 575, 2009 Mich. LEXIS 1115 (2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Workers' Compensation Appellate Commission (Michigan) affirmed a magistrate's decision dismissing plaintiff employee's claim for workers' compensation benefits after he, a professional hockey player, aggravated a preexisting injury when he was struck by another player in a hockey game. After the appellate court denied leave to appeal, the state supreme court ordered oral argument on his application for leave to appeal he filed with it.


OVERVIEW: The employee, a professional hockey player residing in Michigan, signed a contract in Michigan with defendant employer's professional hockey team. Two years later, he injured himself in a fall in New Brunswick, Canada. He stayed for treatment and became a New Brunswick resident. He later aggravated his injury when he was struck in a game. He filed for Michigan's workers' compensation benefits. Even though an existing statute, MCL 418.845, required that a claimant be a Michigan resident and the relevant contract for hire to be made in Michigan, courts had interpreted it as allowing for claims to be brought solely if there was a contract executed in Michigan. The Workers' Compensation Appellate Court affirmed a dismissal of his claim for benefits. While his case was proceeding, the state supreme court in Karaczewski interpreted MCL 418.845 to require both Michigan residency and Michigan contract execution, and gave that decision retroactive effect. The state supreme court, confronted with the employee's case, overruled the retroactive effect of Karaczewski. It found that the parties' reliance interests would not be affected and no undue hardship would result from such a ruling.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the Workers' Compensation Appellate Commission's decision and remanded the case to the Commission with directions to resolve the case consistent with the law in effect before Karaczewski.

 

56 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


O'Neal v. St. John Hosp. & Med. Ctr.


Nos. 138180, 138181


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


487 Mich. 485; 791 N.W.2d 853; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1661



July 31, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: O Neal v. St. John Hosp. & Med. Ctr., 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 2159 (Mich. Ct. App., Nov. 4, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patient appealed the judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals that reversed the trial court's denial of summary disposition to appellees, a doctor and a hospital, on the issue of proximate causation in the patient's medical malpractice suit.


OVERVIEW: The patient argued that the court of appeals erred in treating the patient's claim as a loss-of-opportunity claim instead of a traditional medical malpractice claim and holding that the patient did not raise a genuine issue of fact because the patient could not prove that receiving the alleged appropriate treatment would have decreased his risk of stroke by greater than 50 percentage points. The supreme court agreed. The court of appeals erred by reversing the trial court's denial of summary disposition. The case presented a traditional malpractice claim. It did not present a claim for loss of opportunity. In traditional malpractice cases, the patient was required to prove that the healthcare provider's negligence more probably than not caused the plaintiff's injury. In the instant case, the testimony of the patient's expert witnesses supported the patient's position on proximate causation under MCL 600.2912a. While that testimony was not dispositive, it was sufficient to raise a question of fact to defeat a motion for summary disposition, allowing the issue to be adjudicated on the merits by the trier of fact.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed and the case was remanded to the court of appeals for consideration of the issue not decided on appeal in that court.

Lansing Sch. Educ. Ass'n v. Lansing Bd. of Educ.


No. 138401


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


487 Mich. 349; 792 N.W.2d 686; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1657



July 31, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Costs and fees proceeding at, Motion granted by Lansing Sch. Educ. Ass'n v. Lansing Bd. of Educ., 794 N.W.2d 621, 2011 Mich. LEXIS 387 (Mich., 2011)
Decision reached on appeal by, On remand at Lansing Sch. Educ. Ass'n v. Lansing Sch. Dist. Bd. of Educ., 293 Mich. App. 506; 810 N.W.2d 95; 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 1451 (Mich. Ct. App., Aug. 9, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Lansing Sch. Educ. Ass'n v. Lansing Bd. of Educ., 282 Mich. App. 165, 772 N.W.2d 784, 2009 Mich. App. LEXIS 155 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed a trial court's judgment upon finding that plaintiff teachers and union lacked standing to sue defendants, a school board and school district, for failing to comply with a statutory duty to expel students who had allegedly physically assaulted the teachers. The state supreme court granted the union and teachers' application for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: The teachers, four in all, allegedly were physically assaulted by students. Each of the incidents allegedly was reported to a school administrator. The students supposedly were suspended but not expelled. The teachers and union filed suit against the school board and school district, and alleged that they had a mandatory duty under MCL 380.1311a(1) to expel students who physically assaulted a teacher. The trial court granted the school board and school district's summary disposition motion. The appellate court affirmed on the basis that the teachers and union lacked standing to sue under Lee. The state supreme court overruled Lee and its progency on the ground that it did not represent proper standing doctrine because it departed too dramatically from Michigan's historical standing approach in order to adopt a federal constitutional standing test despite significant differences between the Federal and Michigan Constitutions. Instead, it determined that Michigan should return to a limited, prudential approach asking whether a litigant was a proper party to request adjudication of a particular issue and that, under that approach, the teachers and the union had standing.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the appellate court's judgment and remanded the case to the appellate court for further proceedings.

 

58 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


McCormick v. Carrier


No. 136738


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


487 Mich. 180; 795 N.W.2d 517; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1654



July 31, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: McCormick v. Carrier, 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 620 (Mich. Ct. App., Mar. 25, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party challenged a decision from the Michigan Court of Appeals, which affirmed a trial court's decision that the injured party was unable to recover from defendant indemnitor under MCL 500.3135.


OVERVIEW: The injured party broke his ankle when he was run over by a truck and underwent two surgeries. He was unable to return to the job he held prior to the accident. An attempt to recover under MCL 500.3135 was unsuccessful, and this appeal followed. In reversing, the supreme court overruled the decision in Kreiner v Fischer, 471 Mich. 109, 683 N.W.2d 611 (2004). The supreme court then set forth the proper test for determining if there had been a serious impairment of a bodily function. Under MCL 500.3135(7), there were three prongs necessary to establish such. This analysis had to be conducted on a case-by-case basis. In this case, the supreme court was able to decide whether the threshold was met as a matter of law under MCL 500.3135(2)(a). The injured party showed an objectively manifested impairment of bodily function since he continued to suffer pain and a reduced range of motion that inhibited certain bodily functions. Further, the impaired body functions were important. Finally, the injured party showed that the impairment affected his general ability to lead his normal life because it influenced some of his capacity to live in his normal, pre-incident manner of living.


OUTCOME: The decision from the court of appeals was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

 

59 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Hendee v. Putnam Twp.


Nos. 137446 and 137447


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


486 Mich. 556; 786 N.W.2d 521; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1453



July 15, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Hendee v. Twp. of Putnam, 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 1746 (Mich. Ct. App., Aug. 26, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a zoning case, defendant township sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which granted injunctive relief to plaintiffs, property owners and a potential developer, on their claims that, inter alia, the township's zoning ordinance was exclusionary in violation of former MCL 125.297a. Plaintiffs sought to have their property rezoned from agricultural to residential use.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs had applied to have a 144-acre farm rezoned from agricultural to residential for a 95-unit planned unit development (PUD). While that rezoning request, and a subsequent variance request, were pending, plaintiffs submitted an application to rezone to permit a 498-unit manufactured housing community (MHC). The township would not consider that application because the PUD application was still pending, and plaintiffs withdrew same. However, plaintiffs filed their constitutional claims on the basis of the MHC application. On appeal, the court reversed, finding plaintiffs' claims were not ripe for judicial review as they had not presented the MHC application for consideration to the township. Federal case law indicated that the futility exception in zoning cases did not apply until property owners had submitted at least one meaningful application for a variance from the challenged zoning regulations. Because the ordinance did not expressly prohibit the MHC use, the township had to be afforded the opportunity to determine whether the ordinance was exclusionary in violation of former MCL 125.297a.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' judgment and remanded for entry of a dismissal order consistent with its opinion.

 

60 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Adair v. State


No. 137424, No. 137453


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


486 Mich. 468; 785 N.W.2d 119; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1447; 76 A.L.R.6th 767



July 14, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at Adair v. State, 2012 Mich. App. LEXIS 2203 (Mich. Ct. App., Nov. 6, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Adair v. People, 279 Mich. App. 507, 760 N.W.2d 544, 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 1988 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, public school districts and taxpayers, and defendants, state department of education and state treasurer (state), were granted leave to appeal from a Court of Appeals' (Michigan) decision, which granted a declaratory judgment that the Headlee Amendment to Mich. Const. art. 9, § 29 had been violated but declined to award plaintiffs' attorney's fees under Mich. Const. art. 9, § 32.


OVERVIEW: A portion of the Headlee Amendment to art. 9, § 29 was a prohibition on unfunded mandates (POUM). Plaintiffs claimed that recordkeeping and reporting requirements in Mich. Exec. Order 2000-9 and MCL 388.1752, made necessary to obtain federal funding, violated the POUM provision because no state appropriations had been made to cover the increased costs to the school districts to meet those requirements. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part and remanded. While the state claimed that plaintiffs had the burden of proving the specific increase in costs to be entitled to a declaratory judgment, "net cost" as defined under MCL 21.233 constituted the actual cost to the state if the state were to provide the activity or service mandated as a state requirement; thus, such information would be in the state's possession. While plaintiffs only received declaratory judgment on one of 21 claims, that claim had been litigated for six years and had clearly been sustained. Therefore, plaintiffs were entitled to recover the attorney's fees and costs incurred with respect to that claim pursuant to art. 9, § 32.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the declaratory judgment but reversed the decision regarding attorney's fees and remanded to the court of appeals for a determination of costs and attorney's fees to be awarded to plaintiffs.
