Shepherd Montessori Ctr. Milan v. Ann Arbor Charter Twp.


No. 137443


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


486 Mich. 311; 783 N.W.2d 695; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1123



June 18, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Shepherd Montessori Ctr. Milan v. Ann Arbor Charter Twp., 487 Mich. 859, 784 N.W.2d 825, 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1662 (2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Shepherd Montessori Ctr. Milan v. Ann Arbor Charter Twp., 178 L. Ed. 2d 752, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 96 (U.S., 2011)
Costs and fees proceeding at, Motion denied by Shepherd Montessori Ctr. Milan v. Ann Arbor Charter Twp., 2011 Mich. LEXIS 77 (Mich., Feb. 7, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Shepherd Montessori Ctr. Milan v. Ann Arbor Charter Twp., 280 Mich. App. 449, 761 N.W.2d 230, 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 1751 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff variance applicant challenged a decision from the Michigan Court of Appeals, which, on remand, affirmed its prior decision that a denial of a zoning variance request by defendant township entities violated the applicant's equal protection rights.


OVERVIEW: The applicant contended that, in applying a zoning ordinance relating to the operation of a school in an office park district, the township treated it differently from another entity and violated equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Const. 1963, art 1, § 2. The trial court granted summary disposition to the township, and, after several appeals, the court of appeals determined that the township had violated the applicant's equal protection rights. This appeal followed. In reversing the court of appeals, the supreme court held that the township's consideration of different requests did not constitute different treatment of similarly situated entities. The applicant was not seeking similar treatment; rather, it was asserting religion in an effort to obtain preferential treatment. Because the applicant failed to demonstrate that it was treated differently from other similarly situated entities, the supreme court did not need to apply the rational basis test. Finally, the township did not discriminatorily apply the facially neutral zoning ordinance against the applicant. There was no evidence to support the applicant's claim of religious bias or animus.


OUTCOME: The decision from the court of appeals was reversed, and a trial court's order granting summary disposition to the township entities was reinstated.

 

64 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Feezel


No. 138031


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


486 Mich. 184; 783 N.W.2d 67; 2010 Mich. LEXIS 1096



June 8, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Feezel, 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 2208 (Mich. Ct. App., Nov. 13, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of failing to stop at the scene of an accident that resulted in death and operating a motor vehicle with the presence of a schedule 1 controlled substance in his body, causing death. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant struck and killed a pedestrian. Defendant argued that the trial court erred by failing to instruct the jury on proximate cause and that his conviction of operating a motor vehicle, causing death, based on the presence of 11-carboxy-tetrahydrocannabinol (11-carboxy-THC) in his body was improper. The supreme court agreed. The victim was walking down the middle of the road, with his back to oncoming traffic, and was extremely intoxicated. The trial court abused its discretion by failing to admit evidence of the victim's intoxication because it was relevant to the element of causation in MCL 257.617(3) and MCL 257.625(4) and (8). In addition, defendant's conviction under MCL 257.625(4) and (8) was based on the court of appeal's improper interpretation of MCL 257.625(8). There was no known pharmacological effect of 11-carboxy-THC, 11-carboxy-THC was not a "synthetic" substance and, thus, it was not a schedule 1 controlled substance under MCL 333.7212(1)(d). Because 11-carboxy-THC was not a schedule 1 controlled substance under MCL 333.7212, a person could not be prosecuted under MCL 257.625(8) for operating a motor vehicle with any amount of 11-carboxy-THC in his system.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed. Defendant's convictions were vacated and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the opinion.

66 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Idziak


No. 137301


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


484 Mich. 549; 773 N.W.2d 616; 2009 Mich. LEXIS 1604



July 31, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Idziak, 483 Mich. 885, 759 N.W.2d 401, 2009 Mich. LEXIS 112 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Michigan Court of Appeals that affirmed defendant's sentence following his conviction of armed robbery and possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony, arguing that the sentencing court erred in failing to award jail credit.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court held that defendant, a parolee convicted and sentenced to imprisonment for a felony committed while on parole, was not entitled, under the Michigan jail credit statute, MCL 769.11b, to credit for time served in jail after his arrest on the new offense and before sentencing for that offense. Under MCL 791.238(2), defendant resumed serving his earlier sentence on the date he was arrested for the new criminal offense. As long as time remained on defendant's earlier sentence, he remained incarcerated, regardless of his eligibility for bond or his ability to furnish it. Because defendant was not being held in jail because of being denied or unable to furnish bond, the jail credit statute did not apply. Further, the sentencing court lacked common law discretion to grant credit against defendant's new minimum sentence in contravention of the statutory scheme.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court was affirmed.

 

67 of 1632 DOCUMENTS



In re Servaas


No. 137633


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


484 Mich. 634; 774 N.W.2d 46; 2009 Mich. LEXIS 1603



July 31, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by, Amended by In re Servaas, 485 Mich. 869; 771 N.W.2d 750; 2009 Mich. LEXIS 1957 (Mich., Sept. 11, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Judicial Tenure Commission (JTC) recommended that the Michigan Supreme Court remove respondent, 63rd District Court judge from office for vacating his office, as well as for judicial misconduct involving a comment and two drawings of a sexual nature. The judge filed objections to the master's report.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that a quo warranto action brought by the Attorney General in the Michigan Court of Appeals was the only appropriate and exclusive proceeding to make the preliminary determination regarding whether the judge vacated or unlawfully held his judicial office. All evidence and testimony obtained during the proceeding by the JTC regarding whether the judge had vacated his office was obtained through an unconstitutional process because the JTC had no authority to proceed on the quo warranto issue or to determine whether the judge vacated his office. That determination could only be made by the Court of Appeals in a quo warranto proceeding, which could then be appealed to the supreme court. The supreme court did not have any constitutional authority to grant the JTC the authority and power to decide whether a judge vacated his or her office. The proper forum for the review of the JTC director's actions was the Attorney Grievance Commission (AGC). The judge's conduct did not rise to the level of judicial misconduct beyond requiring public censure. The judge did not intentionally lie under oath.


OUTCOME: The recommendation of the JTC was rejected with respect to the vacation of office claim and the finding that the judge vacated his office was null and void.


 

68 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Hunter v. Hunter


No. 136310


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


484 Mich. 247; 771 N.W.2d 694; 2009 Mich. LEXIS 1602



July 31, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Hunter v. Hunter, 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 602 (Mich. Ct. App., Mar. 20, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant parents appealed a decision of the Michigan Court of Appeals that affirmed a circuit court order awarding custody of the parents' children to plaintiffs, the children's aunt and uncle.


OVERVIEW: The parents signed documents giving limited guardianship of their children to the children's aunt and uncle because the parents were in the throes of cocaine addiction. Subsequently, the aunt and uncle filed suit seeking legal and physical custody of the children. The circuit court awarded custody to the aunt and uncle, finding that the custodial presumption favored the aunt and uncle and that the parents were unfit. On review of the appellate court decision affirming the circuit court order, the court held that the established custodial environment presumption in MCL 722.27(1)(c) had to yield to the parental presumption in MCL 722.25(1) and that the parental presumption could be rebutted by clear and convincing evidence that custody with the natural parents was not in the best interests of the children. Due process did not require a threshold determination of parental fitness in custody cases, and the appellate court's decision in Mason v. Simmons, 267 Mich App 188, 704 NW2d 104 (2005), was overruled.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was reversed, and the case was remanded for a new hearing on the best interests of the children.

 

69 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jackson


No. 135888


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


483 Mich. 271; 769 N.W.2d 630; 2009 Mich. LEXIS 1569



July 10, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Jackson, 483 Mich. 884, 759 N.W.2d 401, 2009 Mich. LEXIS 104 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant began serving a prison term following a nolo contendere plea, the trial court imposed, inter alia, a court-appointed attorney's fee. Defendant's court-appointed appellate counsel moved to correct the sentence, arguing, inter alia, that the attorney fee was imposed without considering his ability to pay. The Court of Appeals of Michigan denied leave to appeal the trial court's denial of the motion. Defendant appealed the order.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the procedure used to impose and enforce the court-appointed attorney fee. People v. Dunbar, 264 Mich. App. 240, 690 N.W.2d 476 (2004), wrongly held that a trial court was required to assess a convicted defendant's ability to pay before imposing such a fee. The ability-to-pay assessment was only constitutionally necessary when that imposition was enforced and a defendant contested his ability to pay. The ability-to-pay assessment was initially obviated under MCL 769.1l, in relation to imprisoned defendants, as the procedure created a presumption of nonindengcy. Here, the trial court did not err by imposing the fee for the court-appointed attorney without conducting an ability-to-pay analysis. Further, it did not err by issuing the remittance order under MCL 769.1l as defendant was presumed to be nonindigent if his prisoner account was only reduced by 50 percent of the amount over $ 50. However, if he contested his ability to pay that amount, he could ask to amend or revoke the remittance order. The trial court then had to decide whether a claim of extraordinary financial circumstances rebutted the statutory presumption of nonindigency.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was affirmed.

Dep't of Human Servs. v. Rood (In re Rood)


No. 136849


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


483 Mich. 73; 763 N.W.2d 587; 2009 Mich. LEXIS 802



April 2, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Roos v. Rood (in Re Rood), 2008 Mich. App. LEXIS 1243 (Mich. Ct. App., Jan. 12, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court terminated respondent father's parental rights to his daughter. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed and remanded the trial court's judgment terminating the father's parental rights because he was not given notice about the proceedings, which violated his due process rights. Appellant, the Michigan Department of Human Services (DHS), sought leave to appeal in the Michigan Supreme Court, and leave was granted.


OVERVIEW: Although the DHS had the father's correct address on file and the trial court used it successfully on one occasion, the trial court reverted, without explanation, to an address where mail had previously been returned as undeliverable. Also, the father was not properly identified, notified, and consulted regarding the initial service plan, the updated service plan, and the hearing to terminate his parental rights as required by MCR 3.965(B)(1) and (E) and MCL 722.954a(2). Furthermore, based on the failure of the trial court and the DHS to notify the father of the proceedings when it was aware of his current address, the father was not provided a meaningful opportunity to participate in the proceedings and he was deprived of his minimal right to procedural due process under the Fourteenth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Const. 1963, art I, § 17. In addition, pursuant to MCL 712A.19b(3)(g) and (j), no one evaluated the father and his lifestyle and whether, based on his past criminal history, he presented a danger to his daughter. Thus, the trial court erred in terminating the father's parental rights to his daughter.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the court of appeals judgment reversing the trial court's order terminating the father's parental rights to his daughter and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings in which the father would have a fair opportunity to participate.

Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Shahideh v. McKee, 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 14950 (6th Cir.) (6th Cir. Mich., 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]

76 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Maxson


No. 129693


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


482 Mich. 385; 759 N.W.2d 817; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 2547



December 22, 2008, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entered a guilty plea to two counts of second-degree criminal sexual conduct. Defendant moved for relief from judgment. The Michigan trial court denied the motion. Defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: After pleading guilty, defendant failed to request appointed counsel or file a direct appeal. At issue was whether the Halbert decision should be applied retroactively to cases in which a defendant's conviction had become final. The supreme court concluded that Halbert did constitute a new rule. The supreme court further concluded that Halbert could not be construed as a watershed decision, neither of the Teague exceptions applied, and Halbert was not retroactive under federal retroactivity jurisprudence. To determine whether Halbert should be applied retroactively under state law, the supreme court considered the following three factors: (1) the purpose of the new rule; (2) the general reliance on the old rule; (3) the effect of retroactive application of the new rule on the administration of justice. The supreme court concluded that although retroactive application of Halbert would potentially provide a small number of defendants with relief, this did not outweigh the certainty that by applying Halbert retroactively many guilty pleading defendants would inundate the appeals process. Halbert should not be applied retroactively to cases in which a conviction had become final.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment of the trial court denying defendant's motion for relief from judgment.

Stone v. Williamson


No. 133986


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


482 Mich. 144; 753 N.W.2d 106; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 1407


January 8, 2008, Argued 
July 24, 2008, Decided 
July 24, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Jackson Circuit Court, Chad C. Schmucker, J. Court of Appeals, SERVITTO, P.J., and TALBOT and SCHUETTE, JJ. (Docket No. 265048).
Stone v. Williamson, 2007 Mich. App. LEXIS 1029 (Mich. Ct. App., Apr. 17, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant health care providers sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan) wherein that court affirmed a jury verdict and award in favor of appellee patient and his wife in a medical malpractice case and found that the requirements of MCL 600.2912a(2) were met for the award.


OVERVIEW: The patient suffered a ruptured aneurysm and underwent emergency surgery, which led to the amputation of both legs. The patient brought this medical-malpractice suit on the theory that a negligent diagnosis resulted in the rupture and all resulting harm. The award from the trial court was approximately $1.9 million. The Court of Appeals affirmed and agreed with the trial court that, on the issue of "loss of opportunity," the patient had met the requirements of MCL 600.2912a(2) because he had gone from a 95 percent chance of attaining a good result to a 10 percent chance of attaining a good result. On further review, the justices affirmed the Court of Appeals decision, but for varying reasons. A majority concluded that this was not a lost opportunity case. From there, the justices had differing opinions as to whether MCL 600.2912a(2) was enforceable and whether the Fulton case was good law with respect to the lost opportunity doctrine. The lead opinion concluded that MCL 600.2912a(2) was unenforceable and that Fulton was not good law. However, because a majority held that this was not a lost opportunity case, the issue of the correctness of Fulton could not be reached.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the Court of Appeals.

Detroit Fire Fighters Ass'n IAFF Local 344 v. City of Detroit


No. 131463


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


482 Mich. 18; 753 N.W.2d 579; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 1404; 184 L.R.R.M. 2802


November 7, 2007, Argued 
July 23, 2008, Decided 
July 23, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wayne Circuit Court, Susan D. Borman, J. Court of Appeals, SCHUETTE, P.J., and BANDSTRA and COOPER, JJ. Supreme Court granted. Supreme Court ordered supplemental briefing, and then ordered reargument. Supreme Court, reversed; injunction vacated; case remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.
Detroit Firefighters Ass'n, I.A.F.F. Local 344 v. City of Detroit, 271 Mich. App. 457, 722 N.W.2d 705, 2006 Mich. App. LEXIS 2110 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city challenged the decision entered by the Court of Appeals (Michigan) that affirmed the trial court's issuance of a preliminary injunction to prevent the implementation of the city's proposed layoff and restructuring plan where plaintiff union contended that the plan violated the status quo provision of 1969 PA 312 (Act 312), MCL 423.243 by, among other things, jeopardizing the safety of the remaining firefighters.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that the circuit court erred when it issued the preliminary injunction preventing the implementation of the restructuring plan. The circuit court issued what amounted to a permanent injunction where the underlying merits of the alleged status quo violation would never be resolved, contrary to the requirements of MCR 3.310(A)(5). The supreme court further held that, when a safety claim was alleged, an employer's challenged action altered the status quo during the pendency of an Act 312 arbitration only if the action was so inextricably intertwined with safety that the action would alter a condition of employment. The trial court had to determine whether the union had satisfied the traditional four-part test for a preliminary injunction, particularly that the union had demonstrated a likelihood of success on the merits that the plan was inextricably intertwined with safety and made a showing of irreparable harm. Second, if the circuit court issued the injunction there had to be a determination on the merits that the challenged employer action was inextricably intertwined with safety.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated the preliminary injunction entered by the circuit court, and remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

Mich. Ass'n of Home Builders v. Dir. of Dep't of Labor & Econ. Growth


No. 135023


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


481 Mich. 496; 750 N.W.2d 593; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 1396



June 25, 2008, Decided 
June 25, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Ingham Circuit Court, Joyce Draganchuk, J. Court of Appeals, TALBOT, P.J., and CAVANAGH and METER, JJ. The Supreme Court, in lieu of granting leave to appeal and without hearing oral argument, affirmed in part and vacated in part.
Mich. Ass'n of Home Builders v. Dir. of Dep't of Labor & Econ. Growth, 276 Mich. App. 467, 741 N.W.2d 531, 2007 Mich. App. LEXIS 2039 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff association appealed a judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals holding that judicial review of an administrative rule was limited to the administrative record and that the circuit court could remand the matter to defendant Department of Labor and Economic Growth for additional investigation or explanation.


OVERVIEW: The association sought judicial review of an administrative determination in a non-contested case. A non-contested case encompassed administrative determinations that did not fall within the definition of a contested case in MCL 24.203(3). The formal procedures in the Administrative Procedures Act, MCL 24.271 through 24.287, applied to contested cases, not to the rulemaking process or the process of judicial review of non-contested cases. Moreover, the APA's provision for expansion of the record in contested cases, MCL 24.305, did not apply to non-contested cases. Accordingly, the court held that judicial review of the association's non-contested case was limited to the administrative record. The administrative record could not be expanded by a remand to the department.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that judicial review of an administrative rule was limited to the administrative record, but the court vacated the part of the court of appeals' judgment holding that the administrative record could be expanded on remand to the department.

85 of 1632 DOCUMENTS



Mich. DOT v. Tomkins


No. 132983


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


481 Mich. 184; 749 N.W.2d 716; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 1162


December 4, 2007, Argued 
June 11, 2008, Decided 
June 11, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Kent Circuit Court, George S. Buth, J. The Court of Appeals, WHITBECK, C.J., and BANDSTRA and MARKEY, JJ. The Supreme Court, reversed and remanded to the circuit court.
Mich. DOT v. Tomkins, 270 Mich. App. 153, 715 N.W.2d 363, 2006 Mich. App. LEXIS 584 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a partial condemnation proceeding, plaintiff state agency appealed a judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals that held that the exclusion of general effects damages in MCL 213.70(2) was unconstitutional because it impermissibly conflicted with the established constitutional meaning of "just compensation."


OVERVIEW: The state agency initiated partial condemnation proceedings against defendant landowners for a highway project. While the landowners did not contest property value, they claimed additional damages to their remaining land attributable to the dust, dirt, noise, vibration, and smell of the highway. The state agency moved for summary judgment, arguing that general effects damages were not recoverable under MCL 213.70(2). The trial court granted summary judgment, but the appellate court reversed. On review, the court held that the appellate court erred in finding MCL 213.70(2) unconstitutional. The phrase "just compensation" was to be given the same meaning as that used by those sophisticated in the law when Const. 1963, art 10, § 2, was ratified in 1963, and there was no reported Michigan case awarding general effects damages before 1963. Because there was negligible direct pre-1963 caselaw or other evidence establishing that the ratifiers of the state constitution in 1963 understood that a taking included the recovery of general effects damages and because there was some evidence to the contrary, there was insufficient evidence to overcome the presumption of constitutionality.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.


 

86 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ream


No. 134913, No. 134925


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


481 Mich. 223; 750 N.W.2d 536; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 1163


December 13, 2007, Argued 
June 11, 2008, Decided 
June 11, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Ream v. Bell, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 33493 ( E.D. Mich., Mar. 13, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Oakland Circuit Court, Rae Lee Chabot, J. The Court of Appeals, FITZGERALD, P.J., and SAWYER and O'CONNELL, JJ. The Supreme Court reversed in part; conviction and sentence reinstated; defendant's application for leave to appeal denied.
People v. Ream, 2007 Mich. App. LEXIS 1856 (Mich. Ct. App., July 31, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence for felony-murder under MCL 750.316(1)(b), but vacated his conviction and sentence for criminal sexual conduct under MCL 750.520b(1) on double-jeopardy grounds. Both the State and defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of assaulting and killing his elderly neighbor. On appeal, the court determined that convicting and sentencing a defendant for both first-degree felony murder and the predicate felony did not necessarily violate the multiple punishments strand of the Double Jeopardy Clause of U.S. Const. amend. V. or Const. 1963, art. 1, § 15. In addition, the court overruled People v Wilder, 411 Mich 328, 308 NW2d 112 (1981) and held that the Blockburger test was applicable to the multiple punishments strand. Under the Blockburger test, convictions of two criminal offenses arising from the same act were prohibited only when the greater offense necessarily included all elements of the lesser offense. Accordingly, conviction of both offenses was precluded only where it was impossible to commit the greater offense without first having committed the lesser offense. Because each of the offenses for which defendant was convicted, felony murder and first-degree criminal sexual conduct, contained an element that the other did not, they were not the same offense and, therefore, defendant could be punished for both.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the part of the appellate court's judgment vacating the first-degree criminal sexual conduct conviction and sentence, reinstated the conviction and sentence, and denied defendant's application for leave to appeal the judgment of the appellate court.

88 of 1632 DOCUMENTS



Nat'l Pride at Work, Inc. v. Governor of Mich.


No. 133429, 133554


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


481 Mich. 56; 748 N.W.2d 524; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 915; 103 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 534; 43 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 2537


November 6, 2007, Argued 
May 7, 2008, Decided 
May 7, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Ingham Circuit Court, Joyce Draganchuk, J. The Court of Appeals, Hoekstra, P.J., and Wilder and Zahra, JJ., reversed. 274 Mich App 147; 732 N.W.2d 139 (2007).
Nat'l Pride at Work, Inc. v. Governor of Mich., 274 Mich. App. 147, 732 N.W.2d 139, 2007 Mich. App. LEXIS 240 (2007)



DISPOSITION:  The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, public employees and their same-sex partners, brought a declaratory judgment action against defendants, the governor and a city, seeking a declaration that Const. 1963, art 1, § 25, did not bar public employers from providing health-insurance benefits to their employees' qualified same-sex domestic partners. The trial court found for plaintiffs, and the Court of Appeals, Michigan, reversed. Plaintiffs and the governor appealed.


OVERVIEW: Const. 1963, art 1, § 25 stated that the union of one man and one woman in marriage was the only agreement recognized as a marriage or similar union for any purpose. The Michigan Supreme Court held that, under that provision, public employers were prohibited from providing health-insurance benefits to their employees' same-sex domestic partners. The pertinent question in determining whether a union was similar to marriage was not whether it gave rise to all the same legal effects as marriage but whether it was being recognized as similar to marriage "for any purpose." Domestic partnerships were similar to marriage because they were the only relationships in Michigan defined in terms of both gender and lack of a close blood connection. When public employers provided health-insurance benefits on the basis of the domestic partnership, they were without a doubt recognizing the partnership. An election brochure indicating that the amendment would not preclude employers from offering benefits to domestic partners could not be used to contradict the unambiguous language of the provision, and other states' provisions were too different for their interpretations to be of persuasive value.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals.


 

89 of 1632 DOCUMENTS



McDonald v. Farm Bureau Ins. Co


No. 132218


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


480 Mich. 191; 747 N.W.2d 811; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 717


October 2, 2007, Argued 
April 23, 2008, Decided 
April 23, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Genesee Circuit Court, Robert M. Ransom, J. The Court of Appeals, SMOLENSKI, P.J., and HOEKSTRA and MURRAY, JJ., affirmed in an unpublished opinion per curiam, issued August 24, 2006 (Docket No. 259168). The Supreme Court granted Farm Bureau's application for leave to appeal. Reversed and remanded for entry of summary disposition in Farm Bureau's favor.
McDonald v. Farm Bureau Gen. Ins. Co., 2006 Mich. App. LEXIS 2601 (Mich. Ct. App., Aug. 24, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured filed an action concerning an underinsured motorist claim. Defendant insurer moved for summary disposition. A trial court denied the insurer's motion and granted summary disposition to the insured. The Court of Appeals, Michigan, affirmed, holding that the trial court had correctly ruled that the contractual limitations period was tolled. The instant court granted the insurer's application for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals affirmed the trial court's decision solely on the basis of its conclusion that the limitations period was tolled pursuant to Tom Thomas. The instant court's caselaw had already declared that Tom Thomas was wrongly decided. The instant court found that express limitations periods in optional insurance contracts were not automatically tolled as a matter of law by filing a claim. Under the plain language of the contract, the insured was required to bring an action against the insurer within one year of an accident, unless she could point to a legally supported reason why that deadline was not effective. The trial court erred in ruling that the phrase "legal action" was ambiguous. Waiver was inapplicable because the insurer did not voluntarily relinquish its right to enforce the one-year time limit. The factual finding that the insurer caused delays was insufficient to grant estoppel because the insured had not shown any reliance on the insurer's conduct or statements. Because the contract unambiguously and not unreasonably required suit to be filed within one year of the accident, the insurer properly denied the claim when the insured failed to meet that deadline.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed. The matter was remanded to the trial court for entry of summary disposition in favor of the insurer.

Toll Northville Ltd. v. Twp. of Northville


No. 132466


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


480 Mich. 6; 743 N.W.2d 902; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 273


October 4, 2007, Argued 
February 5, 2008, Decided 
February 5, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by, Motion granted by, Request denied by, Without prejudice MJC/Lotus Group v. Twp. of Brownstown, 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 2514 (Mich. Ct. App., Feb. 9, 2011)
Appeal after remand at, Remanded by MJC/Lotus Group v. Brownstown Twp., 293 Mich. App. 1, 809 N.W.2d 605, 2011 Mich. App. LEXIS 1001 (2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wayne Circuit Court, John A. Murphy, J. The Court of Appeals, WHITBECK, C.J., and HOEKSTRA and WILDER, JJ., affirmed in an opinion per curiam, concluding that improvements that become part of the real property as structures or fixtures constitute taxable "additions" under the constitution and the statute, but that term does not include public-service improvements because title to the improvements will ultimately vest in the municipality or a utility company.
Toll Northville, Ltd. v. Northville Twp., 272 Mich. App. 352, 726 N.W.2d 57, 2006 Mich. App. LEXIS 2848 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developers sued defendant town, alleging that an increase in their property tax assessments based on the public-service improvements violated Const. 1963, art 9, § 3, as amended by Mich. Proposal A (1994). The Court of Appeals of Michigan affirmed the trial court, concluding that the term additions did not refer to the public-service improvements that were to ultimately vest in the town or a utility company. The town appealed.


OVERVIEW: MCL 211.34d(1)(b)(viii) was unconstitutional because the mere installation of public-service improvements on public property or on utility easements did not constitute a taxable "addition," as that term was understood when the public adopted Const. 1963, art 9, § 3, as amended by Mich. Proposal A (1994) (Proposal A). The definition of "addition" found in MCL 211.34d(1)(a), as adopted by the legislature in 1993, reflected the meaning of the term "addition" as understood by those sophisticated in the law at the time Proposal A was adopted. Thus, the lower court properly held that MCL 211.34d(1)(b)(viii) was unconstitutional because it was inconsistent with the meaning of the term "additions" as used in Proposal A and that public-service improvements did not constitute "additions" to property within the meaning of Proposal A. However, the lower court erred in defining ambiguous. The lower court also mistakenly concluded that taxing property on the basis of the value added by the availability of public services and also taxing utility lines as personal property of the utility companies resulted in double taxation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed to the extent the lower court held that the statute was unconstitutional, but was vacated to the extent the lower court defined the term ambiguous and concluded that taxing property on the basis of the value added by the availability of public services and also taxing utility lines as personal property of the utility companies resulted in double taxation.

Houdini Props., LLC v. City of Romulus


SC: 132018


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


480 Mich. 1022; 743 N.W.2d 198; 2008 Mich. LEXIS 218



January 18, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
COA: 266338. Wayne CC: 05-504139-CZ.
Houdini Props. v. City of Romulus, 2006 Mich. App. LEXIS 1855 (Mich. Ct. App., June 13, 2006).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developer appealed the denial of his request for a billboard variance by the zoning board of appeals (ZBA), claiming it was a taking. The circuit court affirmed the ZBA's decision. In a separate lawsuit, plaintiff sued defendant city seeking damages for a taking. The circuit court granted summary disposition to the city based on compulsory joinder and res judicata. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed. The developer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the developer's claim of appeal pursuant to MCL 125.585(11) was not a "pleading" for purposes of MCR 2.110(A). As the city acknowledged, the joinder rules of MCR 2.203 therefore did not apply to a claim of appeal from the decision of a zoning board of appeals. The decision of the circuit court on appeal from the zoning board of appeals' denial of a use variance was not res judicata on the developer's constitutional claims. The zoning board of appeals did not have jurisdiction to decide the developer's substantive due process and takings claims. Under MCL 125.585(11), the circuit court's review was confined to the record and decision of the zoning board of appeals. Therefore, the circuit court could not rule on takings issues in the developer's appeal. The Court of Appeals and the circuit court erred in relying on the rationale of the unpublished decision in Sammut v City of Birmingham.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appeals court and vacated the circuit court's orders granting summary disposition to the city.

94 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Grebner v. State


SC: 135274


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


480 Mich. 939; 744 N.W.2d 123; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 2894



November 21, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
COA: 281814. Ingham CC: 07-001507-CZ.
Grebner v. State, 277 Mich. App. 220; 744 N.W.2d 203; 2007 Mich. App. LEXIS 2632 (Mich. Ct. App., Nov. 16, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State of Michigan and the Secretary of the State of Michigan, challenged a decision from the Michigan Court of Appeals, which entered judgment in favor of plaintiffs, several objectors and a consulting firm, in a dispute over the constitutionality of MCL 168.615c. A motion for a stay had been filed.


OVERVIEW: MCL 168.615c provided that information collected about voters at a presidential primary was disseminated to political parties. After MCL 168.615c was enacted, it was contended that it violated Const. 1963, art 4, § 30 because there was a vote of less than two-thirds of the members of each house of the legislature. The court of appeals entered judgment for the objectors and the firm, and this appeal followed. In reversing the court of appeals, the supreme court held that there was no appropriation for a private purpose. In this case, political parties unquestionably served a public purpose. Whether the costs of permitting access to that information was warranted in light of the benefits was a matter primarily for a legislative determination. The supreme court disagreed with the court of appeals' determination that the public purpose served was merely incidental. The predominant role played by political parties was to inform the public about candidates and to facilitate public debate in the context of such candidates. Therefore, MCL 168.615c did not violate Const. 1963, art 4, § 30 or Const. 1963, art 2, § 4. Finally, there was standing in the case, and it was ripe for review.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed. The case was remanded to a circuit court for entry of an order denying the motions filed by the objectors and the firm and dismissing the complaint. The motion for a stay was denied as moot.

Goldstone v. Bloomfield Twp. Pub. Library


Docket No. 130150


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


479 Mich. 554; 737 N.W.2d 476; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1629


April 10, 2007, Argued 
July 26, 2007, Decided 
July 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Goldstone v. Bloomfield Twp. Pub. Library, 738 N.W.2d 223; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 2131 (Mich., Sept. 18, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Oakland Circuit Court, Denise Langford Morris, J. The Court of Appeals, OWENS, P.J., and FITZGERALD and SCHUETTE, JJ., affirmed. The Supreme Court granted the plaintiff's application for leave to appeal.
Goldstone v. Bloomfield Twp. Pub. Library, 268 Mich. App. 642, 708 N.W.2d 740, 2005 Mich. App. LEXIS 2750 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the resident of a city without a library, sued defendant, a township's library, seeking a declaratory judgment granting him borrowing rights equivalent to those of a township resident on the basis that such rights were assured by Const. 1963, art 8, § 9. The trial court entered judgment for the library, and the Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court agreed with plaintiff that a public library was only "available" under art 8, § 9, when a person enjoyed reasonable borrowing privileges. However, the court disagreed with plaintiff's premise that each individual public library facility in Michigan had to be "available" on identical terms to all residents of the state. The court construed the provision to mean that it was not each individual library facility that had to be made available, but rather public libraries as an entity or as an institution that had to be made available. The legislature sufficiently met its obligation of making the library institution "available" through the enactment of numerous laws which encouraged local control of libraries and the establishment of a borrowing system. Plaintiff failed to support his argument that his First Amendment right to receive information was violated. His equal protection argument failed because the library's regulations were a reasonable way to achieve its purpose of establishing and maintaining its library.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendant library.

People v. Harper


No. 130988, No. 131898


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


479 Mich. 599; 739 N.W.2d 523; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1627


April 12, 2007, Argued 
July 26, 2007, Decided 
July 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at People v. McCuller, 479 Mich. 672, 739 N.W.2d 563, 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1628 (2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari dismissed by Burns v. Michigan, 128 S. Ct. 1183; 169 L. Ed. 2d 959; 2008 U.S. LEXIS 1218 (U.S., Feb. 7, 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Harper v. Michigan, 128 S. Ct. 1444; 170 L. Ed. 2d 278; 2008 U.S. LEXIS 2015 (U.S., Feb. 25, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Genesee Circuit Court, Geoffrey L. Neithercut.

The Court of Appeals denied Harper's delayed application for leave to appeal.

The Supreme Court granted Harper's application for leave to appeal to consider whether an intermediate sanction constitutes a statutory maximum sentence under Blakely v Washington, 542 US 296; 124 S. Ct. 2531; 159 L. Ed. 2d 403 (2004), for which the factors supporting a sentencing court's departure must be found by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt or admitted by the defendant.

Allegan Circuit Court, Harry A. Beach, J.

The Court of Appeals denied Burns's application for leave to appeal.

The Supreme Court granted leave to appeal to consider whether an intermediate sanction is a statutory maximum sentence under Blakely for which the reasons for a departure must be found by a jury or admitted by the defendant when the defendant is being sentenced for a probation violation.
People v. Harper, 477 Mich. 933, 723 N.W.2d 567, 2006 Mich. LEXIS 2659 (2006)
People v. Burns, 477 Mich. 933, 723 N.W.2d 567, 2006 Mich. LEXIS 2661 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two separate cases, the trial courts departed from the sentencing guidelines recommendations and imposed upon defendants sentences that exceeded the recommendations. The Court of Appeals of Michigan denied defendants' applications for leave to appeal their sentences. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants argued that because the guidelines minimum sentence ranges called for intermediate sanctions under MCL 769.31(b), 769.34(4), the intermediate sanctions became the relevant statutory maximum sentence under Blakely, and therefore they were constitutionally entitled to such sanctions, and therefore the trial courts erred by sentencing them to indeterminate prison terms. On appeal, the court disagreed and affirmed. The court held that because Michigan had a true indeterminate sentencing system in which the sentencing guidelines were used only to determine a defendant's minimum sentence, an intermediate sanction did not constitute a maximum sentence under Blakely. The court affirmed the first defendant's sentence because his record of bench warrants, his three parole revocations, and his history of absconding from parole constituted substantial and compelling reasons to sentence him to the jurisdiction of the Department of Corrections. The court affirmed the second defendant's sentence because he admitted that he had touched a young woman's buttocks and then harassed and intimidated the two young women while on probation.


OUTCOME: Defendants' convictions and sentences were affirmed.

People v. McCuller


No. 128161


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


479 Mich. 672; 739 N.W.2d 563; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1628



July 26, 2007, Decided 
July 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McCuller v. Mich., 128 S. Ct. 1871; 170 L. Ed. 2d 751; 2008 U.S. LEXIS 3115 (U.S., Apr. 14, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Oakland Circuit Court, Richard D. Kuhn, J.
McCuller v. Michigan, 127 S. Ct. 1247, 167 L. Ed. 2d 62, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 2002 (U.S., 2007)
People v. Harper, 479 Mich. 599, 739 N.W.2d 523, 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1627 (2007)
People v. McCuller, 475 Mich. 176, 715 N.W.2d 798, 2006 Mich. LEXIS 1199 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: One of three companion cases involving the application of Blakely v. Washington to Michigan's sentencing scheme was returned to the court following a remand from the United States Supreme Court for further consideration in light of the Court's holding in Cunningham v. California.


OVERVIEW: The Cunningham opinion did not alter the court's view that Michigan's statutory scheme required the sentencing court to score both offense variables (OVs) and prior record variables (PRVs) before determining the defendant's minimum sentence. A defendant's qualification for an intermediate sanction was contingent on the sentencing court's calculation and application of such sentencing variables. A sentencing court's fact-finding in scoring the OVs did not increase a defendant's statutory maximum under Blakely. The proper scoring of both the OVs and the PRVs did not place defendant in an intermediate sanction cell. Instead, defendant's scores placed him in a "straddle cell" with a maximum sentence of 15 years in prison. Defendant was sentenced within that statutory maximum. Michigan had a true indeterminate sentencing scheme. Even if defendant were to be sentenced on the basis of his PRV score alone, the sentencing court would not have violated Blakely by sentencing him to the statutory maximum of 15 years in prison. Moreover, any such error would have been harmless. The factors underlying the scoring of the OVs were uncontested and supported by overwhelming evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the original sentence.

 

99 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Keller


No. 131223, No. 131224


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


479 Mich. 467; 739 N.W.2d 505; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1624



July 25, 2007, Decided 
July 25, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Genesee Circuit Court with maintaining, Geoffrey L. Neithercut, J. The Court of Appeals consolidated the appeals and, in a split decision, Davis, P.J., and Cavanagh, J. (Talbot, J., dissenting), held that the evidence should be suppressed under the exclusionary rule because the search warrant and the underlying affidavit could not support a finding of probable cause and that the good-faith exception to the exclusionary rule did not apply.
People v. Keller, 270 Mich. App. 446, 716 N.W.2d 311, 2006 Mich. App. LEXIS 920 (2006).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Michigan holding that defendants' motion to suppress drug evidence seized from their home should have been granted because the search warrant affidavit was insufficient.


OVERVIEW: After an anonymous tip that defendants were operating a marijuana growing and distribution operation out of their home, police officers conducted a trash pull, in which they discovered a partially burnt marijuana cigarette, a green leafy substance, and correspondence. While executing a search warrant, the officers seized six ounces of marijuana, firearms, and marijuana smoking paraphernalia. On appeal, the court held that the search warrant was constitutional under the Fourth Amendment because, even accepting the appellate court's determination that the anonymous tip was unreliable, the marijuana from the trash provided a substantial basis for concluding that probable cause existed, and therefore the evidence found in defendants' home was not subject to the exclusionary rule. The court held that there was no violation of MCL 780.653 because the search warrant affidavit was based on the affiant's personal efforts to search the trash and discover the marijuana because that evidence was the foundation for probable cause. While the anonymous tip prompted the officers' investigation of defendants, the affidavit was not based on that information but rather was based on the trash pull.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Trentadue v. Gorton


No. 128579, Nos. 128623, 128624, and, 128625


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


479 Mich. 378; 738 N.W.2d 664; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1622


December 12, 2006, Argued 
July 25, 2007, Decided 
July 25, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Trentadue v. Buckler Automatic Lawn Sprinkler Co., 480 Mich. 1202; 739 N.W.2d 79; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 2507 (Mich., Sept. 28, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Genesee Circuit Court, Robert M. Ransom, J. The Court of Appeals consolidated appeals by leave granted filed by Trentadue, Buckler, Shirley and Laurence Gorton, and MFO, and the Court, Owens, P.J., and Sawyer and White, JJ., affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded the matter to the trial court, holding that the common-law discovery rule tolled the limitations period for all of the plaintiff's claims. 266 Mich App 297; 701 N.W.2d 756 (2005).
Trentadue v. Buckler Automatic Lawn Sprinkler Co., 266 Mich. App. 297, 701 N.W.2d 756, 2005 Mich. App. LEXIS 1070 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the personal representative of the landlord's estate and a management company, appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Michigan that reversed summary judgment under MCR 2.116(C)(7) that was entered for defendants in a wrongful death action filed by plaintiff personal representative of the victim's estate on the ground that the common-law discovery rule tolled the limitations period for all of plaintiff's claims.


OVERVIEW: The victim, who rented a house on the landlord's property, was raped and murdered by the employee of a company that serviced the sprinkler system. Sixteen years later after the murderer was identified plaintiff sued defendants. The appellate court denied defendants summary judgment, holding that the three-year statute of limitations of MCL 600.5805(10) was tolled by the common-law discovery rule. On appeal, the court held that the plain language of MCL 600.5827 precluded the use of a common-law discovery rule to the accrual date of claims to which the statute applied. Because the wrong was done when the victim was raped and murdered in 1986, and MCL 600.5827 was in effect at that time, plaintiff's claims accrued at the time of her death, and therefore were barred by the three-year statute of limitations. The court did not deprive plaintiff of due process by holding that the claims were time-barred because the statutes furthered a legitimate legislative aim, namely relieving defendants of the burden of defending claims brought after the time established. Because plaintiff did not detrimentally rely on confusing pre-existing case law, the court declined to employ equitable tolling.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

 

102 of 1632 DOCUMENTS



Mich. Citizens for Water Conservation v. Nestle Waters N. Am., Inc.


Nos. 130802, 130803


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


479 Mich. 280; 737 N.W.2d 447; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1626



July 25, 2007, Decided 
July 25, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Mich. Citizens for Water Conservation v. Nestle Waters N. Am., Inc., 480 Mich. 1203, 739 N.W.2d 332, 2007 Mich. LEXIS 2501 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Mecosta Circuit Court, Lawrence C. Root, J.
Mich. Citizens for Water Conservation v. Nestle Waters N. Am., Inc., 269 Mich. App. 25, 709 N.W.2d 174, 2005 Mich. App. LEXIS 2940 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  The court affirmed the appeals court except with regard to the issue of plaintiffs' standing to bring a MEPA claim regarding certain wetlands under dispute, and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Judgment was entered for plaintiff, a citizens group and its members, including certain property owners, on their groundwater claim and Michigan Environmental Protection Act (MEPA) claim against defendants, lessors and a water bottling company. The company was enjoined from further water pumping activities. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed, reversed, and remanded. Both plaintiff and the company appealed. Limited review was granted.


OVERVIEW: Review was limited to whether plaintiffs had standing to bring a claim under MEPA as that claim related to certain streams, lakes, and wetlands. Plaintiffs indisputably had standing to bring a MEPA claim as to those areas in which the property owners enjoyed riparian rights, and the individual members with riparian rights conferred organizational standing on the group. However, plaintiffs had no use, access, nor recreational, aesthetic, or economic interest in other areas, and thus, they had no substantial interest in those areas detrimentally affected by the company's pumping activities that was distinct from the interest of the general public. The absence of a concrete, particularized injury in fact was fatal to their standing. The court rejected the "interconnectedness" theory of standing. No matter how pervasive the environmental damage in an ecosystem, plaintiffs still had to successfully and succinctly establish their injury in fact. Neither Const 1963, art 4, § 52, nor MCL 324.1701(1) lightened the burden to satisfy traditional standing requirements. The potential effect of the remedy was not to be confused with plaintiffs' constitutional burden to prove standing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appeals court except with regard to the issue of plaintiffs' standing to bring a MEPA claim regarding certain wetlands under dispute, and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.


 

103 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nyx


No. 127897


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


479 Mich. 112; 734 N.W.2d 548; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1581


November 14, 2006, Argued 
July 18, 2007, Decided 
July 18, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Nyx, 480 Mich. 1204; 739 N.W.2d 89; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 2532 (Mich., Oct. 5, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Wayne Circuit Court, Vera Massey Jones, J. The Court of Appeals, Neff, P.J., and Cooper and R.S. Gribbs, JJ., vacated the convictions and remanded the matter to the trial court for the entry of an order of acquittal of the charges of CSC I. Unpublished opinion per curiam, issued January 13, 2005 (Docket No. 248094). The Supreme Court granted the prosecution's application for leave to appeal. 474 Mich 1099; 711 N.W.2d 83 (2006).
People v. Nyx, 2005 Mich. App. LEXIS 53 (Mich. Ct. App., Jan. 13, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two counts of second-degree criminal sexual conduct in violation of MCL 750.520c(1)(b)(iii). The Court of Appeals of Michigan reversed, holding that under MCL 768.32(1), defendant could not be convicted of the lesser-include offense of second-degree criminal sexual conduct (CSC II) after the trial court acquitted him of first-degree criminal sexual conduct (CSC I) under MCL 750.520b. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for sexually assaulting the victim, a student. Defendant was charged with CSC I. After acquitting defendant of that charge, it sua sponte convicted defendant of CSC II. On appeal, the court held that CSC II was not a necessarily included lesser offense of CSC I because the elements of CSC II were not all subsumed within CSC I, as CSC II required proof of one of several intents that were not always present when CSC I was committed; therefore, CSC II was a cognate lesser offense. MCL 768.32(1) precluded a judge or jury from convicting a defendant of a cognate lesser offense even if the crime was divided into degrees because the word "inferior" in MCL 768.32(1) was best understood as meaning an offense that was necessarily included in the greater charge. Because all of the elements of CSC II were not included within CSC I, the trial court was without authority to convict defendant of CSC II after it acquitted him of CSC I. Therefore, the appellate court properly vacated defendant's conviction and remanded the case for an order of discharge.


OUTCOME: The judgment reversing defendant's convictions was affirmed and the case was remanded to the trial court for an order of discharge.

 

104 of 1632 DOCUMENTS



In re Request for Advisory Opinion Regarding Constitutionality of 2005 PA 71


No. 130589


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


479 Mich. 1; 740 N.W.2d 444; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1582


November 13, 2006, Argued 
July 18, 2007, Decided 
July 18, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
The House of Representatives, pursuant to Const 1963, art 3 § 8, requested the opinion of the Supreme Court regarding the constitutionality of 2005 PA 71, MCL 168.523, which requires that, before voting, voters either present photo identification or sign an affidavit averring that the voter lacks photo identification. The Supreme Court entered an order granting the request and requesting the Attorney General to submit separate briefs arguing that the photo identification requirements of 2005 PA 71, are, and are not, constitutional.
In re Request for Advisory Opinion re Constitutionality of 2005 PA 71, 474 Mich. 1230, 712 N.W.2d 450, 2006 Mich. LEXIS 737 (2006).



DISPOSITION: The statute was facially constitutional under the federal and state Equal Protection Clauses as well as the Twenty Fourth Amendment.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan House of Representatives requested an advisory opinion on the constitutionality of MCL 168.523. Of concern was the constitutionality of the requirement that voters either present photo identification or sign an affidavit averring that the voter lacks photo identification before voting.


OVERVIEW: MCL 168.523(1) was facially constitutional under the United States Constitution because the obligation of either presenting photo identification or signing an affidavit was not a severe burden on the right to vote. The statute imposed only a reasonable, nondiscriminatory restriction on the election process in furtherance of Michigan's compelling regulatory interest in preventing voter fraud and enforcing Const. 1963, art 2, § 4, to preserve the purity of elections and to guard against abuses of the elective franchise by ensuring that lawful voters did not have their votes diluted. Michigan's Equal Protection Clause did not compel that every election regulation be reviewed under strict scrutiny. Rather, the flexible Burdick test was used as it struck the appropriate balance between protecting a citizen's right to vote and protecting against fraudulent voting. Because no voter was required to incur the costs of obtaining a photo identification card as a condition of voting, the statute did not impose the payment of a fee as a condition to the exercise of the franchise and therefore was not an unconstitutional poll tax under U.S. Const. amend. XXIV.


OUTCOME: The statute was facially constitutional under the federal and state Equal Protection Clauses, as well as the Twenty-Fourth Amendment.

People v. $ 180,975 in United States Currency (In re Forfeiture of $ 180,975)


No. 127983


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


478 Mich. 444; 734 N.W.2d 489; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1511


March 6, 2007, Argued 
July 3, 2007, Decided 
July 3, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Van Buren Circuit Court, William C. Buhl, J. The Court of Appeals, Markey, P.J., and Fitzgerald and Owens, JJ., affirmed in an unpublished memorandum opinion, issued December 28, 2004 (Docket No. 249699). The Supreme Court granted Smith's application for leave to appeal, directing the parties to address the proper application of the exclusionary rule in a forfeiture proceeding in which the property subject to forfeiture has been illegally seized and whether In re Forfeiture of United States Currency, 166 Mich App 81; 420 N.W.2d 131 (1988), was correctly decided.
People v. Smith (in Re Forefeiture of $180,975.00), 2004 Mich. App. LEXIS 3574 (Mich. Ct. App., Dec. 28, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed a circuit court's decision that appellee state presented a preponderance of untainted evidence that supported a finding of civil forfeiture of the claimant's cash, pursuant to MCL 333.7521(1)(f).


OVERVIEW: The claimant was stopped for speeding. A trooper searched the trunk and found $ 180,975 in cash in a backpack. The claimant was cited for speeding and driving on a suspended license. The state filed a complaint for forfeiture of the cash. Evidence of the backpack and its contents were suppressed on the basis that their seizure was illegal. The court agreed with the court of appeals that the circuit court did not clearly err in finding that, although the cash was illegally seized, there was a preponderance of untainted evidence to support a finding of civil forfeiture, pursuant to MCL 333.7521(1)(f), including the claimant's unexplained and repeated history of using rental cars and her inconsistent testimony as to why she had such a large amount of cash in the rental car in relation to her reported income and while traveling a known drug corridor. The exclusionary rule was not meant to immunize illegally seized property from a civil forfeiture proceeding involving that property. The court held that, in accord with MCL 333.7521, as long as the forfeiture was established by a preponderance of evidence untainted by the illegal search and seizure, the forfeiture was valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Greater Bible Way Temple v. City of Jackson


Nos. 130194, 130196


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


478 Mich. 373; 733 N.W.2d 734; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1346


November 13, 2006, Argued 
June 27, 2007, Decided 
June 27, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Greater Bible Way Temple v. City of Jackson, 480 Mich. 1201, 738 N.W.2d 222, 2007 Mich. LEXIS 2130 (2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Greater Bible Way Temple v. City of Jackson, 128 S. Ct. 1894, 170 L. Ed. 2d 782, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 3441 (U.S., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Jackson Circuit Court, Chad C. Schmucker, J. The Court of Appeals, Fort Hood, P.J., and Meter and Schuette, JJ., affirmed, holding that the defendants had violated RLUIPA and that the application of RLUIPA to compel the requested rezoning did not render the statute unconstitutional. The Supreme Court granted the defendants' application for leave to appeal.
Greater Bible Way Temple v. City of Jackson, 268 Mich. App. 673, 708 N.W.2d 756, 2005 Mich. App. LEXIS 2788 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff church filed a complaint against defendant, a city, planning commission, and city council, containing two counts challenging the city's zoning decision and alleging a violation of Religious Land Use and Institutionalized Persons Act of 2000 (RLUIPA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000cc et seq. The trial court granted the city's motion for summary disposition with regard to count one; that decision was not appealed. Leave to appeal was granted.


OVERVIEW: The church wanted to build an apartment complex across the street from its church on property that it owned in the city. There were single-family residences on each side of the property. The issue presented was whether 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000cc et seq., entitled the church to the rezoning of its property from single-family residential to multiple-family residential to allow the church to build an apartment complex. The next question was whether the building of an apartment complex constituted a religious exercise. The supreme court concluded that a refusal to rezone did not constitute an individualized assessment, and, thus, that RLUIPA was inapplicable. Further, even if RLUIPA was applicable, the building of an apartment complex did not constitute a religious exercise. Even if it did constitute a religious exercise, the city's refusal to rezone the church's property did not substantially burden the church's religious exercise. If it did substantially burden religious exercise, the imposition of that burden was in furtherance of a compelling governmental interest and constituted the least restrictive means of furthering that interest. The RLUIPA was not violated.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court, and the case was remanded to the trial court for the entry of a judgment in favor of defendants.

Taxpayers of Mich. Against Casinos v. State


Docket Nos. 129816, 129818, 129822


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


478 Mich. 99; 732 N.W.2d 487; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1253


October 5, 2006, Argued  
May 30, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Ingham Circuit Court, Peter D. Houk, J.
Taxpayers of Mich. against Casinos v. State, 268 Mich. App. 226, 708 N.W.2d 115, 2005 Mich. App. LEXIS 2246 (2005).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer group challenged a decision from the Michigan Court of Appeals, which, on a remand, entered judgment for defendants, the State, an Indian tribe, and a gaming company, by determining that several gaming compacts violated Const. 1963, art 3, § 2. The group also challenged the decision to strike a portion of their brief dealing with Const. 1963, art 9, § 17.


OVERVIEW: The Governor of Michigan and four Indian tribes signed gaming compacts. The group challenged the constitutionality of the compacts, and the supreme court remanded the case to the court of appeals to determine if an amendatory provision violated the separation powers doctrine in Const. 1963, art 3, § 2. On remand, the court of appeals determined that it was violated. This appeal followed. In partially reversing, the supreme court determined that the amendatory provision of the compacts and the Governor's exercise of that provision did not violate the separation of powers clause because the amendatory provision was properly approved by legislative resolution. Moreover, the Governor's use of the amendatory provision was exercised within the constitutional limits. The issue of whether the tribal payments under the compacts violated Const. 1963, art 9, § 17 was not properly before the supreme court because it was beyond the parameters of the supreme court's prior remand order.


OUTCOME: The portion of the decision from the court of appeals that struck the parts of the brief dealing the appropriations issue was affirmed. The rest of the decision was reversed. The case was remanded to a circuit court for an entry of summary disposition against the taxpayer group.

Wayne County Treasurer v. Perfecting Church (In re Treasurer of Wayne Foreclosure)


No. 129341


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


478 Mich. 1; 732 N.W.2d 458; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1002


October 5, 2006, Argued  
May 23, 2007, Decided  
May 23, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application denied by Wayne County Treasurer v. Precision Materials & Servs. (In re Petition for Foreclosure), 479 Mich. 859, 735 N.W.2d 286, 2007 Mich. LEXIS 1673 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wayne Circuit Court, Mary Beth Kelly, J. The Court of Appeals, FORT HOOD, P.J., and TALBOT and MURRAY, JJ., denied the intervening parties' delayed application for leave to appeal. Unpublished order of the Court of Appeals, entered July 11, 2005 (Docket No. 261074).
Wayne County Treasurer v. Perfecting Church (In re Treasurer of Wayne County for Foreclosure), 474 Mich. 1059, 711 N.W.2d 297, 2006 Mich. LEXIS 246 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant intervening parties sought review of an order of the Wayne Circuit Court (Michigan), which restored respondent church's title to real property after the property was foreclosed upon and sold by the county to the intervening parties.


OVERVIEW: The church did not receive notice of the county's foreclosure of its property because the treasurer did not comply with the notice provisions of the General Property Tax Act (GPTA), MCL 211.1 et seq., but sent notice to a previous owner. The court affirmed the circuit court's decision to grant the church relief from a foreclosure judgment granted by the circuit court to the county. The court held that, because there was no construction of the GPTA that rendered the statute constitutional in cases where the taxing authority denied the taxpayer due process, the statute was unconstitutional as applied to those individuals. The court held that the jurisdictional limitation of MCL 211.78k(6) unconstitutionally encompassed all foreclosures, including those where there was no constitutionally adequate notice. The court held that the county completely failed to comply with the notice provisions in the GPTA and, as such, deprived the church of its property without providing due process under the Due Process Clause of the United States Constitution and of the Michigan Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Rowland v. Washtenaw County Rd. Comm'n


No. 130379


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


477 Mich. 197; 731 N.W.2d 41; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 985


October 4, 2006, Argued  
May 2, 2007, Decided  
May 2, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] The trial court, David S. Swartz, J. The Court of Appeals, DONOFRIO, P.J., and ZAHRA, J. (KELLY, J., concurring), affirmed in an unpublished opinion per curiam, issued December 13, 2005 (Docket No. 253210).
Rowland v. Washtenaw County Rd. Comm'n, 2005 Mich. App. LEXIS 3089 (Mich. Ct. App., Dec. 13, 2005).



DISPOSITION: The Supreme Court granted the defendant's application for leave to appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A trial court denied defendant county road commission's motion for summary disposition of plaintiff pedestrian's personal injury action filed under MCL 691.1402 on the ground that the pedestrian failed to serve notice on the commission within 120 days of the injury as required by MCL 691.1404(1). The commission appealed and the Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirmed. The commission appealed.


OVERVIEW: The pedestrian fell and was injured while crossing an intersection in the county. She served her notice on the commission 140 days after her fall. The commission moved for summary disposition on the ground that notice was not filed within 120 days as required by MCL 691.1404(1). The trial court denied the motion, holding that there was a genuine issue of material fact concerning whether the commission had shown prejudice by the late notice. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that MCL 691.1404 should be enforced as written and overruled prior cases that held that absent a showing of actual prejudice to the government agency, failure to comply with the notice provision was not a bar to claims filed under the defective highway exception. The court held that MCL 691.1404(1) was not unconstitutional because it did not require that the government be prejudiced by late notice. Because the statute was constitutional, engrafting the prejudice requirement onto it was unlawful. The court further held that its decision had full retroactive effect. Because the pedestrian failed to provide notice within 120 days of her fall, her claim was barred by MCL 691.1404(1).


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded with directions to grant the commission's motion for summary disposition.

Fluor Enters. v. Revenue Div., Dep't of Treasury


No. 129149


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


477 Mich. 170; 730 N.W.2d 722; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 976


October 5, 2006, Argued  
May 2, 2007, Decided  
May 2, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Fluor Enters. v. Mich. Dep't of Treasury, 128 S. Ct. 391; 169 L. Ed. 2d 264; 2007 U.S. LEXIS 11436 (U.S., Oct. 9, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Claims, Paula J. Manderfield, J. The Court of Appeals, Cavanagh, P.J., and Jansen and Gage, JJ. The Supreme Court granted the defendant's application for leave to appeal and the plaintiff's application for leave to appeal as cross-appellant.
Fluor Enters. v. Dep't of Treasury, 265 Mich. App. 711, 697 N.W.2d 539, 2005 Mich. App. LEXIS 909 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sued defendant Revenue Division, Department of Treasury, State of Michigan, challenging taxes allegedly owed under the Single Business Tax Act, MCL 208.1 et seq. The Michigan Court of Appeals held that services performed outside of Michigan for construction projects located in Michigan were taxable thereunder, but that the statute violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8 cl. 3. The parties cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer asserted that MCL 208.53(c) deemed receipts for services taxable as Michigan receipts only if the services were performed within Michigan. The state supreme court disagreed, however, finding that the statute established a two-part analysis. The taxpayer engaged in architectural and engineering services for Michigan construction projects. Because those activities constituted "services performed" for "construction activities," and those "construction activities" took place "within this state," the State could tax the services. The taxpayer's attempt to construe its services as planning or design activities failed to recognize that it performed its services for construction activities within Michigan. Moreover, the supreme court held that the statute was constitutional. The receipts at issue were for services provided for Michigan projects; thus, the incidence of the tax, as well as its measure, was tied to the earnings that the State had made possible, meaning that there was a substantial nexus between the state and the activity being taxed. Further, because § 208.53 did not discriminate against interstate commerce, it did not violate the "internal consistency" test.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed in part but reversed in part with regard to the analysis of fair apportionment. It remanded to the state appellate court for further proceedings.

Griev. Adm'r v. Fieger


SC: 127547


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


477 Mich. 1228; 729 N.W.2d 451; 2006 Mich. LEXIS 3037



December 21, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by Griev. Adm'r v. Fieger, 729 N.W.2d 215; 2007 Mich. LEXIS 706 (Mich., Apr. 6, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ADB: 01-55-GA.
Griev. Adm'r v. Fieger, 476 Mich. 231, 719 N.W.2d 123, 2006 Mich. LEXIS 1470 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court previously determined that U.S. Const. amend. I did not bar the application of the Michigan Rules of Professional Conduct to statements made by respondent attorney in the course of a pending lawsuit in the court of appeals. As a result, the attorney was to be reprimanded but allowed to continue his practice of law. The attorney moved for a "stay" of the judgment pending his application for certiorari with the U.S. Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: The attorney's motion had to be denied simply because there was no evidence whatsoever that he would suffer any "irreparable injury" by the court's failure to grant a stay. The attorney could continue to practice law during his application for certiorari and, if he prevailed in the United States Supreme Court, his reprimand would be undone. Under the court's very clear historical practice, stays were granted under only the most truly extraordinary circumstances.


OUTCOME: The attorney's motion for a stay of proceedings was denied.
