Silver Creek Drain Dist. v. Extrusions Div., Inc.


No. 119721


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


468 Mich. 367; 663 N.W.2d 436; 2003 Mich. LEXIS 1221


November 19, 2002, Argued  
June 17, 2003, Decided  
June 17, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Silver Creek Drain Dist. v. Extrusions Div., Inc., 668 N.W.2d 145, 2003 Mich. LEXIS 1727 (Mich., 2003)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Extrusions Div., Inc. v. Silver Creek Drain Dist., 157 L. Ed. 2d 893; 124 S. Ct. 1062; 2004 U.S. LEXIS 89 (U.S., Jan. 12, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Kent Circuit Court, Donald A. Johnston, J. Court of Appeals, SMOLENSKI, P.J., and ZAHRA and COLLINS, JJ. 245 Mich App 556 (2001).
Silver Creek Drain Dist. v. Extrusions Div., Inc., 245 Mich. App. 556, 630 N.W.2d 347, 2001 Mich. App. LEXIS 86 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals' judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part; remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed in part the trial court's condemnation award to appellee landowner and held that the Uniform Condemnation Procedures Act (UCPA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 213.51 et seq., gave no authority to a court to consider any contamination factor in the establishment of fair market value. The court granted leave to appeal to consider appellant drain district's claim to the contrary.


OVERVIEW: The landowner operated a plastics extruding business and owned a parcel of land adjacent to its plant. The landowner was denied a permit to build on the parcel, as the district wanted to build a storm-water retention pond thereon. In considering the value of the land, the trial court took into account the contamination factor of the land and determined the reasonable cost of the land accordingly. The appellate court reversed in part and disagreed with the trial court's valuation. The court reversed on appeal. The court considered the meaning of the term "just compensation" as used in Mich. Const. art. X, § 2. The court found that the term was a phrase of art that meant that the proper amount of compensation for property took into account all factors relevant to market value, and it was this meaning that was attributed to the drafters of state constitution and the UCPA. The appellate court erred in analyzing the term under the plain language doctrine. Thus, the trial court properly considered the contamination factor in determining the fair market value of the parcel, and further proceedings were ordered.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the part of the appellate court's decision that held that the UCPA did not vest courts with the authority to consider contamination and how it affected fair market value in a condemnation proceeding. The court affirmed the remainder of the decision and remanded for further proceedings.

People v. Clay


No. 120024


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


468 Mich. 261; 661 N.W.2d 572; 2003 Mich. LEXIS 980


December 11, 2002, Argued  
May 30, 2003, Decided

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Clay, 468 Mich. 1249, 2003 Mich. LEXIS 2949 (2003)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Clay v. Birkett, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 23370 ( E.D. Mich., Sept. 23, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Kent Circuit Court, H. David Soet, J. Court of Appeals, NEFF, P.J., and SMOLENSKI and ROBERSON, JJ. (Docket No. 183102). Court of Appeals, REILLY, P.J., and MACKENZIE and ZAHRA, JJ. (Docket No. 183101). Court of Appeals, HOOD, P.J., and FITZGERALD, J., (HOLBROOK, JR., J., dissenting). 239 Mich. App. 365, 608 N.W.2d 76 (2000). Supreme Court. 463 Mich. 971, 623 N.W.2d 597 (2001). Court of Appeals. 247 Mich. App. 322, 636 N.W.2d 303 (2001).
People v. Clay, 247 Mich. App. 322, 636 N.W.2d 303, 2001 Mich. App. LEXIS 174 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals' decision reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate cases, defendant was convicted of assault of a correction officer and carrying a concealed weapon without a permit and, in both cases, of being a habitual offender. The convictions in one case were reversed. Defendant's motion for post-conviction relief as to the other convictions was denied. After remand, the Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed the trial court's denial his motion for relief from judgment, and the State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped for allegedly trespassing and committing other violations, and the officers discovered a concealed weapon. During the resulting incarceration, defendant assaulted a corrections officer. Defendant's weapons charge and one habitual offender charge were reversed due to lack of probable cause for the initial stop. Based on that reversal, defendant sought relief from the conviction for the assault charge, claiming that he was not lawfully imprisoned, as required by Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.197c. The court held that the reversal of defendant's conviction for the weapon offense, reversed by application of the exclusionary rule, did not mean that defendant was not lawfully imprisoned, as contemplated by Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.197c. The concealed weapon offense was committed in an officer's presence; therefore, Mich. Comp. Laws § 764.15(1) authorized the officer to arrest defendant on that charge. The court rejected the argument that the finding of lack of probable cause for the stop retroactively rendered the arrest or its subsequent imprisonment unlawful.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and reinstated the trial court's denial of defendant's motion for relief from judgment.

 

158 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Sweatt v. Dep't of Corr.


No. 120220


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


468 Mich. 172; 661 N.W.2d 201; 2003 Mich. LEXIS 919


November 20, 2002, Argued  
May 13, 2003, Decided  
May 13, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Worker's Compensation Appellate Commission affirmed the magistrate's decision. Court of Appeals, NEFF, J. (WHITE, J., concurring) (GRIFFIN, P.J., dissenting). 247 Mich App 555; 637 N.W.2d 811 (2001).
Sweatt v. Dep't of Corr., 247 Mich. App. 555, 637 N.W.2d 811, 2001 Mich. App. LEXIS 194 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed the judgment of the Workers' Compensation Appellate Commission granting judgment in favor of plaintiff employee by concluding that the fact that the employee was no longer able to work for defendant employer as a result of the employee's commission of a crime did not relieve the employer of its responsibility to pay the employee differential benefits. The employer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employer argued that it did not have to pay the employee differential workers' compensation benefits, under § 361 of the Worker's Disability Compensation Act (WDCA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.101 et seq., where the employer could not rehire the employee because of the employee's commission of a crime. The appellate court noted that, pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 791.205a(1), the employee had been convicted of a felony, thus, the employer could not reemploy the employee. Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.361(1) of the WDCA only applied to employees who were able to work in some capacity. It only addressed those situations in which the employee was employed, but earning less money than before the work-related injury. Employers had to compensate employees for a loss of wage-earning capacity that resulted from a work-related injury; but, if the difference in pay was because of imprisonment or commission of a crime, the employee was not entitled to differential benefits. The employee's lost earning capacity was caused not by a work-related injury, but the commission of a felony. The WDCA was not intended to compensate employees for committing crimes and becoming imprisoned.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded to the magistrate to determine what extent, if any, the employee's loss of wage-earning capacity was attributable to his work-related injury and to what extent, if any, the employee's loss of wage-earning capacity was attributable to his commission of a crime.

Kenneth Henes Special Projects Procurement, Mktg., & Consulting Corp. v. Cont'l Biomass Indus. (In re Certified Question)


Docket No. 120110.


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


468 Mich. 109; 659 N.W.2d 597; 2003 Mich. LEXIS 772


November 20, 2002, Argued  
April 23, 2003, Decided  
April 23, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] United States District Court for the Eastern District of Michigan, Southern Division, Gerald E. Rosen, J. United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit, Keith, Kennedy, and Batchelder, JJ.
Kenneth Henes Special Projects Procurement v. Continental Biomass Indus., 86 F. Supp. 2d 721, 2000 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 1499 (E.D. Mich., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Certified questions were answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff representative filed an action against defendant corporation, which sought to recover unpaid sales commissions and penalty damages pursuant to the Michigan Sales Representative Commission Act (SRCA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.2961. The United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit certified a question to the court regarding the imposition of double-damages under the SRCA.


OVERVIEW: The representative sold equipment for the corporation under the terms of a contract. After the representative was terminated, he sought unpaid commissions on four sales. The corporation refused to pay because it did not believe that the representative was entitled to the commissions. During the trial in federal court, the corporation sought a jury instruction regarding the mental state required for the double-damages provision contained in the SCRA. The federal trial court refused to give the instruction. On appeal, the federal appellate court certified a question to the court regarding the intent required under the SCRA. The court determined that evidence of bad faith was not required in order to recover double-damages under the SCRA. The court determined that the clear language of the SCRA did not show an intent to create a good faith defense to the double-damages provision of Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.2961(5). Because there was no ambiguity in § 600.2961(5), the court was not permitted to examine the legislative history. The court noted that the only cognizable defense to a double-damages claim was that the failure to pay the commissions was based on inadvertence or oversight.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the plain language of the SCRA did not require evidence of bad faith before double-damages could have been imposed. The case was returned to the federal court for further proceedings.

Taylor v. Smithkline Beecham Corp.


Nos. 120624, 120637, 120638, 120639, 120640, 120641, 120642, 120643, 120644, 120645, 120646, 120653, 120654

468 Mich. 1; 658 N.W.2d 127; 2003 Mich. LEXIS 458


December 10, 2002, Argued

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wayne Circuit Court, Marianne O. Battani, J. Washtenaw Circuit Court, David S. Swartz, J. Court of Appeals, MURPHY and JANSEN, JJ. (COLLINS, P.J., did not participate). 248 Mich App 472; 639 N.W.2d 45 (2001).
Taylor v. Gate Pharms., 248 Mich. App. 472, 639 N.W.2d 45, 2001 Mich. App. LEXIS 227 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals decision reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant drug companies sought review of a decision of the Michigan Court of Appeals, which held Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.2946(5) unconstitutional on the grounds that it represented an unconstitutional delegation of authority by the state legislature.


OVERVIEW: The drug companies were the subjects of product liability actions in the Circuit Courts of Wayne County and Washtenaw County, based on the companies' manufacture and distribution of diet drugs. In each lawsuit, the companies moved for summary judgment based on Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.2946(5), which limited the companies' liability, as the drugs at issue were approved for safety and efficacy by the United States Food and Drug Administration (FDA) and labeled in compliance with FDA standards. The Wayne County Circuit Court held that the statute was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority. The other trial court held the statute to be constitutional. The Court of Appeals agreed with the Wayne County Court and held that statute improperly granted the FDA the authority to determine when a drug manufacturer would be liable under Michigan law. The Michigan Supreme Court held that the statute did not improperly delegate legislative authority to the FDA, as the act of approving a drug as safe had significance independent of its effect on Michigan law. Dearborn Independent, Inc. v Dearborn, 331 Mich. 447; 49 N.W.2d 370 (1951), was overruled.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Court of Appeals was reversed.

In re 2002 Pa 48 House of Representatives' Request


SC: 121394


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


467 Mich. 1203; 652 N.W.2d 667; 2002 Mich. LEXIS 1627



September 23, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of May 28, 2002 is VACATED and the Court respectfully declines to issue an advisory opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court of Michigan previously granted a request by the Michigan House of Representatives for an advisory opinion on the constitutionality of certain provisions of the Metropolitan Extension Telecommunications Rights-of-Way Oversight Act (2002 PA 48). Amicus curiae briefs were filed. Motions by interest groups were pending before the supreme court.


OVERVIEW: Although the supreme court invited anyone with an interest in 2002 PA 48 to file amicus curiae briefs on the questions posed by the Michigan House of Representatives, only one of the five briefs filed presented a challenge to the constitutionality of the act based upon only one of the questions at issue. Given the apparent lack of interest in the legal questions posed by the legislature concerning 2002 PA 48 and the absence of briefing sufficient to raise and flesh out potential competing arguments and contexts necessary to a reasoned opinion, the supreme court concluded that the citizens of Michigan would be better served to have any challenges to 2002 PA 48 resolved in the traditional manner.


OUTCOME: The motion by the Michigan Cable Telecommunications Association for leave to file a brief amicus curiae was granted. The motion by the Michigan Municipal League and the Michigan Townships Association for oral argument was denied as moot. The supreme court vacated its earlier order and declined to issue an advisory opinion to the legislature.

 

164 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wayne County Clerk


Docket Nos. 121918, 121919, 121938, 121939.


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


466 Mich. 640; 648 N.W.2d 202; 2002 Mich. LEXIS 1241



July 17, 2002, Decided  
July 17, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wayne Circuit Court, Timothy M. Kenny, J. Court of Appeals, KELLY, P.J., and WILDER and MARKEY, JJ., in Docket No. 242227, and TALBOT, P.J., and WILDER and MARKEY, JJ., in Docket No. 242278.
State v. Wayne County Clerk, 649 N.W.2d 73, 2002 Mich. LEXIS 1237 (Mich., 2002)



DISPOSITION: On application by the defendants for leave to appeal, the Supreme Court, in lieu of granting leave, reversed the judgment of the circuit court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a mandamus action filed by plaintiffs, the circuit court ordered that a proposition changing the current at-large system of electing the Detroit city council to a single-member district plan be placed on the ballot. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) denied the motions of the intervenors, the Detroit City Council and its incumbent members, and defendant, the Detroit Election Commission, to keep the proposition off the ballot. They appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Election Commission declined to certify the proposition for inclusion on the ballot. Mich. Const. art. 4, § 29 provided that a local act could not take effect until approved by two-thirds of the members of each house and a majority of electors voting in the district affected. Although 2002 Mich. Pub. Act 432, which would amend the Home Rule City Act by adding Mich. Comp. Laws § 117.3a, did not refer by name to Detroit, but rather purported to apply to any city with a population of more than 750,000 that had a 9-member at-large elected city council, only Detroit met that population criterion. Thus, the statute plainly failed to qualify as a general act. Even if another city reached a population of 750,000 and had a 9-member at-large council, 2002 Mich. Pub. Act 432 would not apply because of its requirement that the proposition appear on the ballot at the August 6, 2002, election. No other city could meet that requirement, as there would be no new census before that date. Because 2002 Mich. Pub. Act 432 did not receive a two-thirds vote in each house, it did not satisfy the requirements for placing the proposition on the ballot.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the circuit court and ordered that plaintiffs' complaint for mandamus be dismissed.

Crawford v. Dep't of Civil Serv.


No. 114930


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


466 Mich. 250; 645 N.W.2d 6; 2002 Mich. LEXIS 1041; 89 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 180



June 4, 2002, Decided  
June 4, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Saginaw Circuit Court, Patrick M. Meter, J. Court of Appeals, JANSEN, P.J., and SAWYER, J. (MARKMAN, J., dissenting), (Docket No. 205603).

CRAWFORD v. DEP'T OF CIV. SERV., 2000 Mich. LEXIS 578 (Mich. Apr. 18, 2000).
CRAWFORD v. DEPARTMENT OF CIV. SERV. & DEP'T OF CORRECTIONS, 1999 Mich. App. LEXIS 546 (Mich. Ct. App., June 1, 1999)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sued defendants, the departments, the commission, and the director, under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, Mich. Const. art. 11, § 5, and the Michigan Civil Rights Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 37.2101 et seq. The Saginaw Circuit Court granted summary disposition against the employee, and the Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirmed.


OVERVIEW: The employee was a corrections officer. He took a written test for supervisors, and received a score of 99 out of 100, which gave him a highly qualified rating. He applied for a lateral transfer to another prison, and was required to apply as if he were seeking a promotion to that position. Ultimately, he was denied the position, which was given to a minority whose test rating was lower than the employee's rating. The employee sued several government entities, alleging that he was improperly denied a promotion and that minority applicants with lower test scores were promoted. The employee lost his case in two courts, and he appealed from those judgments. The supreme court held that: (1) the "safe harbor" provision of Mich. Comp. Laws § 37.2210 did not bar the employee's constitutional or federal law claims; (2) the employee had standing to pursue those claims; and (3) further factual development was needed on the merits, making summary disposition inappropriate under Mich. Ct. R. 2.116(C)(10).


OUTCOME: The judgments were reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

Danse Corp. v. City of Madison Heights


No. 119011


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


466 Mich. 175; 644 N.W.2d 721; 2002 Mich. LEXIS 946



May 29, 2002, Decided  
May 29, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Danse Corp. v. City of Madison Heights, 466 Mich. 1222, 649 N.W.2d 77, 2002 Mich. LEXIS 1278 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, MURPHY and FITZGERALD, JJ. (SAWYER, P.J., dissenting), (Docket No. 215486).
DANSE CORP. v. CITY OF MADISON HTS., 2001 Mich. App. LEXIS 1058 (Mich. Ct. App., Mar. 23, 2001)



DISPOSITION: Judgments of the Tax Tribunal and Court of Appeals reversed and remanded to the Tax Tribunal for reconsideration in light of this opinion. MCR 7.302(F)(1).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed a judgment that personal property belonging to petitioner corporation did not constitute "special tools" and was therefore subject to taxation. The corporation appealed, arguing that guidelines utilized by the tax tribunal were not determinative, since they were not rules promulgated in accordance with the Administrative Procedures Act (APA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 24.201 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The corporation used plastic injection molds and related components. The state tax commission held that the molds were not exempt "special tools" within the meaning of Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.9b. The supreme court held that the plain language of Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.10e demonstrated a legislative intent that the state tax commissioner's assessor's manual was to be used "as a guide." It could not be concluded that the legislature intended that the state tax commission was entitled to bypass the APA and promulgate the assessor's manual as a rule having the force of law. Mich. Admin. Code r. 21 established two requirements for qualification for the special tools exemption. First, the special tool had to be "used" or be "being prepared for use" in manufacturing. Second, the special tools had to be of such a specialized nature that their utility and amortization ceased with the discontinuance of such products. The assessor's manual guidelines contained a short useful life requirement, but Mich. Admin. Code r. 21 did not allow that construction. Rule 21 only addressed whether the tool would have usefulness after the product was discontinued. There was no other time condition.


OUTCOME: The judgments of the tax tribunal and court of appeals was reversed and the case was remanded to the tax tribunal for reconsideration in light of the supreme court's opinion.

 

167 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Wpw Acquisition Co. v. City of Troy


No. 118750


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


466 Mich. 117; 643 N.W.2d 564; 2002 Mich. LEXIS 749


January 23, 2002, Argued  
May 14, 2002, Decided  
May 14, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Oakland Circuit Court, Joan E. Young, J. Court of Appeals, MARKEY, P.J., and GRIBBS and GRIFFIN, JJ., 243 Mich App 260, 620 N.W.2d 883 (2000) (Docket No. 211828).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee city sought review in the Michigan Court of Appeals of the trial court's decision reversing the Michigan Tax Tribunal's ruling in favor of the city with regard to its assessment of the taxpayer's property. The court of appeals reversed the trial court's decision, and the taxpayer appealed.


OVERVIEW: A constitutional amendment limiting real property tax increases was adopted, providing a property's value could be adjusted for "additions," without regard to the amendment's cap. The legislature adopted Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.34d(1)(b)(vii), defining "additions" to include an increase in a property's value due to increased occupancy by tenants. The supreme court held that when the amendment was passed, the General Property Tax Act defined "additions" as increases in a property's value caused by new construction or the addition of equipment or furnishings, or the value of property exempt from or not included in a preceding year's assessment, in Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.34d(1)(a), as then in effect. This did not include an increase in occupancy by tenants. The term "additions" in the constitutional amendment was to be given the definition understood at the time of enactment, as the amendment did not intend another meaning. The legislature could not redefine constitutional terms. The attempt, in Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.34d(1)(b)(vii), to expand the meaning of "additions," for property tax valuation purposes, was inconsistent with the constitutional amendment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed.

Pohutski v. City of Allen Park


No. 116949, No. 117935


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


465 Mich. 675; 641 N.W.2d 219; 2002 Mich. LEXIS 487


October 11, 2001, Argued  
April 2, 2002, Decided  
April 2, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wayne Circuit Court, Edward M. Thomas, J. Court of Appeals, WILDER, P.J., and HOOD and ZAHRA, JJ. Docket No. 222238. Oakland Circuit Court, Jessica R. Cooper, J. Court of Appeals, FITZGERALD, P.J., and JANSEN, J. (DOCTOROFF, J., not participating). Docket No. 227657.



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners, in two separate actions, filed class action suits against defendant city for trespass, nuisance, trespass-nuisance, negligence, and unconstitutional taking. The Wayne Circuit Court (Michigan) granted the homeowners' summary disposition motions on the trespass-nuisance claims. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: The city experienced a "10 year storm" in February 1998. As a result of the high volume of rainfall, raw sewage from the city's sewer system backed up through the homeowners' floor drains and into their basements. The appellate court held that under a plain reading of Mich. Comp. Laws Serv. § 691.1407, the first sentence of § 691.1407 applied to both municipal corporations and the state, while the second sentence applied only to the state. The plain language of § 691.1407 did not contain a trespass-nuisance exception to governmental immunity and trespass-nuisance simply was not one of the five exceptions to immunity set forth in § 691.1407. The court overruled a prior decision that held that trespass-nuisance was a recognized exception to the governmental immunity statute. However, the effect of its rule only operated prospectively, such that the homeowners were not precluded from seeking recovery. The court also held that Mich. Pub. Act § 222 also was not retrospective in its application.


OUTCOME: The matter was remanded to the circuit courts to reconsider the homeowners' motions for summary disposition under prior case law.

Le Roux v. Sec'y of State


No. 120338


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


465 Mich. 594; 640 N.W.2d 849; 2002 Mich. LEXIS 464


January 23, 2002, Argued  
March 25, 2002, Decided  
March 25, 2002, Filed



DISPOSITION: The [***1] application for review of the redistricting plan is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff voters filed an original action in the Michigan Supreme Court challenging the plan of the defendant secretary of state and director of elections' for redistricting Michigan's 15 seats in the United States House of Representatives adopted by 2001 Mich. Public Acts 115.


OVERVIEW: In support of their action, the voters claimed that redistricting plan of 2001 Mich. Pub. Acts 115 was not validly enacted, as the Secretary of the Senate, prior to presentation of the bill to the Governor for his approval, changed the bill by inserting references to exclude census tracts. The voters also contended that the plan failed to comply with statutory requirements for congressional redistricting established by 1999 Mich. Pub. Acts 221. The supreme court rejected the voters' arguments. The court concluded that 2001 PA 115 was validly enacted, as the changes made before submission to the Governor were technical corrections that did not violate the provisions of the Michigan Constitution regarding enactment of legislation. The court also held that the requirements of 1999 Mich. Pub. Acts 211 was not binding on the Legislature's redistricting of Michigan's congressional seats in 2001, and therefore the reference to the 1999 guidelines in 2001 Mich. Pub. Acts 115's redistricting plan did not indicate an intention by the Legislature to make the redistricting plan reviewable using those guidelines.


OUTCOME: The court denied the voter's application for review of the congressional redistricting plan.

 

170 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hamilton


No. 118615


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


465 Mich. 526; 638 N.W.2d 92; 2002 Mich. LEXIS 35



January 23, 2002, Decided  
January 23, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Livingston Circuit Court, Stanley J. Latreille, J. Court of Appeals, DOCTOROFF, P.J., and HOEKSTRA and MARKEY, JJ., (Docket No. 225712).
PEOPLE v. HAMILTON, 2001 Mich. App. LEXIS 562 (Mich. Ct. App., Jan. 26, 2001)



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeals and remand the case to the Livingston Circuit Court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged a Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirmation of a Livingston Circuit Court (Michigan) dismissal of a drunk driving charge against defendant because the arrest was made by a police officer acting outside his jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court found that the fact that an arrest was made by an officer outside his jurisdiction did not require exclusion of the evidence obtained as a result of the arrest or dismissal of the charge. The officer lacked the statutory authority to make the arrest under Mich. Comp. Laws § 764.16. The officer did have probable cause to arrest the defendant based on sobriety tests and this meant that the arrest did not violate the Fourth Amendment protection against unreasonable seizures. Where the officer had probable cause to believe that the crime had been committed, and therefore had the constitutionally required basis to search and seize, there would appear to be no need to suppress such evidence, even though the arrest was statutorily illegal. Because Mich. Comp. Laws § 764.2a did not require exclusion of evidence obtained as a result of police conduct that was not in accordance with the statute, the circuit court erred in suppressing the evidence and dismissing the charges against defendant.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed and remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings.

Muskegon Area Rental Ass'n v. City of Muskegon


No. 118416


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


465 Mich. 456; 636 N.W.2d 751; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 2463



December 18, 2001, Decided  
December 18, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Muskegon Circuit Court, Timothy G. Hicks, J. Court of Appeals, JANSEN and WHITBECK, JJ. (BANDSTRA, P.J., dissenting in part). (Docket No. 217854).

Muskegon Area Rental Ass'n v. City of Muskegon, 244 Mich. App. 45, 624 N.W.2d 496, 2000 Mich. App. LEXIS 275 (2000) 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Muskegon, Mich., Code of Ordinances § 4-83(b), provided that before a residential dwelling may be rented, all past due property taxes on the property had to be paid. Plaintiffs, rental property owners, brought an action challenging the validity of the ordinance. The Muskegon Circuit Court, granted summary disposition for defendant city on all the claims, but the Court of Appeals, Michigan, reversed in part.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals found that the city ordinance violated the Equal Protection Clauses of U.S. Const., amend. XIV, and Mich. Const. art. 1, § 2. On review, the Supreme Court held that: (1) the property owners failed to meet their burden to show that the ordinance was not rationally related to the legitimate objectives of the ordinance; (2) as long as the ordinance was rationally related to the city's legitimate purposes, it did not violate equal protection guarantees; and (3) because the property owners did not overcome their considerable burden of demonstrating that the city lacked any rational basis for its ordinance, the city was entitled to summary disposition on the equal protection claim.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed in part, and the judgment of the circuit court was reinstated.

People v. Jackson


No. 117594


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


465 Mich. 390; 633 N.W.2d 825; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1865



September 25, 2001, Decided  
September 25, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 9, 2001.
Rehearing denied by, Amended by People v. Jackson, 465 Mich. 1209, 2001 Mich. LEXIS 2244 (2001)


PRIOR HISTORY: Saginaw Circuit Court, Robert S. Gibson, J. Court of Appeals, SHEPHERD, P.J., and WAHLS and FORD, JJ. (Docket No. 86292). Supreme Court, 432 Mich. 896 (1989) Court of Appeals, MARKEY, P.J., and BANDSTRA and NEFF, JJ. (Docket No. 225416).
People v. Jackson, 2000 Mich. App. LEXIS 2872 (Mich. Ct. App., July 18, 2000)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), denying his motion for relief from judgment following his conviction for first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty following a bench trial of first-degree murder, and his conviction was affirmed on appeal. He filed a motion for relief from judgment under Mich. Ct. R. 6.500. Relief was denied by the circuit court, and the court of appeals denied leave to appeal. The matter was put before the state supreme court. In addition to arguing the substantive issues, defendant maintained that limitations on relief provided by Mich. Ct. R. 6.508(D) were inapplicable because his conviction predated the effective date of the rule. The state supreme court disagreed. Application of Rule 6.508(D) did not bar the constitutional prohibition on ex post facto laws. Further, Rule 6.508(D)(3) procedure was not a firmly established and regularly followed rule of the Michigan courts at the time of defendant's conviction. Thus, it did not constitute an adequate and independent state ground barring review of defendant's habeas petition in federal court.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Mich. Coalition of State Emple. Unions v. Mich. Civil Serv. Comm'n


No. 115579


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


465 Mich. 212; 634 N.W.2d 692; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1209


April 4, 2001, Argued  
July 27, 2001, Decided  
July 27, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Ingham Circuit Court, James R. Giddings, J. Court of Appeals, GRIBBS, P.J., and KELLY and HOOD, JJ. 236 Mich App 96 (1999) (Docket No. 212236).



DISPOSITION: Reverse the Court of Appeals in part, vacate the preliminary injunction entered by the circuit court in its entirety, and remand case to circuit court for any appropriate proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the Michigan Civil Service Commission, adopted amended Mich. Civ. Serv. R. 406. The Ingham Circuit Court, Michigan, granted a preliminary injunction to plaintiffs (unions) to prohibit the commission from implementing the amended rule. The court of appeals granted the commission's application for leave to appeal from the issuance of the preliminary injunction, and affirmed in part and reversed in part. The commission appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the Supreme Court of Michigan considered the question whether a party alleging a violation of Mich. Const. art 11, § 5, the provision of the Michigan Constitution related to the state civil service system, must make a particularized showing of irreparable harm to obtain a preliminary injunction against the alleged violation. The supreme court concluded that the lower courts erred in viewing a particularized showing of irreparable harm as unnecessary to obtaining a preliminary injunction against an alleged violation of Mich. Const. art 11, § 5. The supreme court read nothing in § 5 that would suggest that, in the civil service realm, the actions of the government were any more susceptible to preliminary injunctive relief than were the actions of any other private or public entity. Pending the resolution of a suit claiming a violation of § 5, a party to such a suit could obtain a preliminary injunction only after satisfying all the requirements traditionally required for this extraordinary relief. Thus, the circuit court abused its discretion by granting a preliminary injunction in the present case in the absence of such a showing.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed in part, the preliminary injunction entered by the circuit court was vacated in its entirety, and the case was remanded to the circuit court.

People v. Vasquez


No. 116660


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


465 Mich. 83; 631 N.W.2d 711; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1212


March 7, 2001, Argued  
July 27, 2001, Decided  
July 27, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Isabella County Trial Court, Paul H. Chamberlain, C.J. Court of Appeals, FITZGERALD, P.J., and SAAD and WHITBECK, JJ. 240 Mich App 239 (Docket No. 222895).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with being a minor in possession of alcohol, Mich. Comp. Laws § 436.1703(1), and resisting and obstructing a police officer, Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.479. The trial court dismissed the obstructing charge. The Michigan Court of Appeals reversed, and defendant filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: The obstructing charge was based on defendant's lying to the police about his identity after he was arrested. The court of appeals based its holding on People v. Philabaun (Philabaun II), where it was held that a defendant's mere refusal to obey an order for a blood sample could constitute resisting or opposing. The Supreme Court of Michigan reversed, finding that the legislature had demonstrated in § 750.479 a purpose of proscribing only conduct amounting to actual or threatened physical interference. The principal purpose of statute was to protect officers from physical harm. Defendant's lie to the police officer about his name and age, while certainly not laudatory, did not physically interfere with or threaten to physically interfere with the officer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and reinstated the trial court's order dismissing the obstruction charge.

Lewis v. State


No. 114241


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


464 Mich. 781; 629 N.W.2d 868; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1188


November 14, 2000, Argued  
July 17, 2001, Decided  
July 17, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Lewis v. State, 467 Mich. 893, 653 N.W.2d 408, 2002 Mich. LEXIS 1727 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Shiawassee Circuit Court, Gerald D. Lostracco, J. Court of Appeals, MARKEY, P.J., and MCDONALD and HOEKSTRA, JJ.
Lewis v. State, 1998 Mich. App. LEXIS 2781 (Mich. Ct. App., Nov. 16, 1998)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, trooper, brought the instant case to seek money damages from appellee, State, for violation of his right under the Michigan Constitution to be free of race and sex discrimination by the State. The trial court ruled in favor of the trooper on his claim for money damages for violation of the Michigan Constitution, awarding damages of over $ 300,000. The Court of Appeals of Michigan peremptorily reversed the trial court.


OVERVIEW: The Michigan Supreme Court refused to recognize a private cause of action against the State for money damages or other compensatory relief for past violations of Mich. Const. art. 1, § 2 because: (1) the plain language of the constitutional provision left its implementation to the legislature; and (2) it was the pattern of the Michigan Constitution with regard to the protection of civil rights to provide the legislature with authority to create remedial measures. The supreme court disagreed with the reliance by the appellate court on the potential availability of a remedy under the Civil Rights Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 37.2101 et seq.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Sharp v. City of Lansing


No. 116171


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


464 Mich. 792; 629 N.W.2d 873; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1191


November 14, 2000, Argued  
July 17, 2001, Decided  
July 17, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Ingham Circuit Court, Peter D. Houk, J. Court of Appeals, TALBOT, P.J., and MARKEY, J. and FITZGERALD, J. 238 Mich App 515 (1999). (Docket No. 205160).



DISPOSITION: Decisions of the trial court and the Court of Appeals dismissing plaintiff's CRA claims affirmed. The Court of Appeals decision on the constitutional claim reversed and remanded to the trial court for consideration of plaintiff's prayer for injunctive relief under art 1, § 2.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff applicant brought a reverse discrimination claim against defendant city. The applicant alleged violations of the Michigan Civil Rights Act (CRA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 37.2101 et seq., and the Equal Protection Clause of the Michigan Constitution, Mich. Const. art. 1, § 2 . The Circuit Court, Ingham County, Michigan, granted summary disposition for the city. The court of appeals affirmed. The applicant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The applicant was a Caucasian male who believed that he was qualified to be a firefighter with the city. He applied for a firefighter position six consecutive years, and each time he was denied employment. The applicant believed that the city wrongfully refused to hire him because he was white. On appeal, the applicant challenged the use of an affirmative action plan in hiring decisions. The supreme court held that Mich. Comp. Laws § 37.2210 barred statutory liability under the CRA for employment discrimination where an employer acted in accordance with an affirmative action plan properly approved by the commission. The decisions of circuit court and the court of appeals dismissing the CRA claims were affirmed. The existence of the safe harbor did not abrogate rights guaranteed under the Equal Protection Clause of the Michigan Constitution. The circuit court erred by dismissing the constitutional claim for injunctive relief. Moreover, the applicant preserved that claim for appellate review.


OUTCOME: The decisions of the lower courts as to the CRA claims were affirmed. However, the court of appeals' decision on the constitutional claim was reversed, and remanded for consideration as to the applicant's request for injunctive relief under the state constitution.

People v. Herron


No. 114858


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


464 Mich. 593; 628 N.W.2d 528; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1157


October 11, 2000, Argued  
July 3, 2001, Decided  
July 3, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Herron v. Straub, 138 Fed. Appx. 753, 2005 U.S. App. LEXIS 14040 (6th Cir.) (6th Cir. Mich., 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wayne Circuit Court, Susan Bieke Neilson, J. Court of Appeals, D. E. HOLBROOK JR., P.J., and FITZGERALD, J. and WHITE, J. (Docket No. 198353).
PEOPLE v. HERRON, 1999 Mich. App. LEXIS 1583 (Mich. Ct. App., Apr. 6, 1999)



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeals and reinstate defendant's conviction and sentence for involuntary manslaughter. However, because defendant received inappropriate multiple punishments for involuntary manslaughter and negligent homicide, we vacate his conviction and sentence for negligent homicide.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with second-degree murder, operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol and a controlled substance causing death, and driving on a suspended license. He was convicted of negligent homicide and driving with a suspended license. He was retried on the murder charge, and convicted of involuntary manslaughter. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) vacated that conviction, and affirmed the one for negligent homicide.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the supreme court considered whether: (1) the retrial for second-degree murder, after a jury in the first trial deadlocked on that count, was an impermissible successive prosecution; (2) the conviction of involuntary manslaughter on retrial resulted in an unconstitutionally impermissible multiple punishment because defendant had previously been convicted of negligent homicide under prosecution for a separate count; and (3) defendant was entitled to a conviction of the lesser offense when multiple punishments resulted from the retrial. The supreme court held: (1) the retrial for second-degree murder was permissible and did not violate the constitutional protection against successive prosecutions; (2) the retrial of defendant resulted in multiple punishments for the same offense; (3) the constitutional violation should have been remedied by affirming the involuntary manslaughter conviction and vacating defendant's conviction of negligent homicide; and (4) Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.33 did not apply because defendant was not subjected to a subsequent trial for different degrees of the same offense for which he was originally acquitted or convicted.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the conviction and sentence for involuntary manslaughter were reinstated. However, because defendant received inappropriate multiple punishments for involuntary manslaughter and negligent homicide, the conviction and sentence for negligent homicide were vacated.

TIG Ins. Co. v. Revenue Div.


No. 115915, No. 115916


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


464 Mich. 548; 629 N.W.2d 402; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1158


March 6, 2001, Argued  
July 3, 2001, Decided  
July 3, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Claims, Michael G. Harrison, J. Court of Appeals, NEFF, P.J., and HOOD and MURPHY, JJ. 237 Mich App 219 (1999) (Docket Nos. 206999, 207443).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs insurance companies sought refunds of their retaliatory tax payments which were denied by the defendant Michigan Department of Revenue. The court of claims reversed the denial of the refunds, and the Michigan Court of Appeals upheld the reversal. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The companies argued that Mich. Comp. Laws 500.476a, and 1988 Mich. Pub. Acts 349 violated the Equal Protection clauses of U.S. Const. Amend. XIV, and Mich. Const. art. I, § 2, and the Uniformity of Taxation Clause, Mich. Const. art. IX, § 3. They contended that the State had exceeded its authority to treat foreign corporations differently than domestic corporations because the different treatment did not bear a rational relation to a legitimate state purpose, and 1988 Mich. Pub. Acts 349, which excluded certain payments from the companies' burdens for retaliatory tax calculations, converted the retaliatory tax from a tax intended to discourage other states from imposing excessive levels of taxation on in-state insurers to a tax designed to raise revenue at the expense of foreign insurers. The supreme court held that neither Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.476a, nor 1988 Mich. Pub. Acts 349 were unconstitutional because they bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state interest, and the companies did not show that the legislation was based solely on reasons totally unrelated to the pursuit of the State's goals.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed.

People v. Carpenter


No. 115617


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


464 Mich. 223; 627 N.W.2d 276; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1106


January 16, 2001, Argued  
June 12, 2001, Decided  
June 12, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Carpenter, 631 N.W.2d 343, 2001 Mich. LEXIS 1206 (Mich., 2001)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Saginaw Circuit Court, William A. Crane. Court of Appeals, NEFF, P.J., and HOOD and MURPHY, JJ. (Docket No. 204051).
People v. Carpenter, 1999 Mich. App. LEXIS 194 (Mich. Ct. App., July 16, 1999)



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Court of Appeals affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was convicted in the Saginaw (Michigan) Circuit Court of first-degree home invasion and felonious assault. The Court of Appeals affirmed, and appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant presented evidence at trial that, although not legally insane, he lacked the mental capacity to form the specific intent required for the crimes. In affirming the conviction, the court of appeals rejected defendant's argument that the trial court erred in shifting the burden to defendant to prove his claim of diminished capacity by a preponderance of the evidence. The supreme court also affirmed. The court agreed with appellant that there was no indication in Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.21a that the legislature intended to make diminished capacity an affirmative defense. However, the court declined to adopt an alternative defense to legal insanity that could swallow up the insanity defense and its attendant commitment provisions. By enacting a comprehensive statutory scheme setting forth the requirements for and the effects of asserting a defense based on either mental illness or mental retardation, the legislature had signified its intent not to allow a defendant to introduce evidence of mental abnormalities short of legal insanity to avoid or reduce criminal responsibility by negating specific intent.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed.

In re Chmura


No. 117565


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


464 Mich. 58; 626 N.W.2d 876; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 887


December 14, 2000, Argued  
May 30, 2001, Decided  
May 30, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Tenure Commission. Supreme Court appointed former Wayne Circuit Judge John P. Kirwan to serve as master. Supreme Court remanded 461 Mich 517 (2000).



DISPOSITION: JTC's recommendation to suspend respondent from all judicial activities without pay for a period of ninety days rejected.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Judicial Tenure Commission (Commission) filed a complaint alleging respondent judge violated Mich. Code Jud. Conduct Canon 7(B)(1)(d) during an election campaign. The Michigan Supreme Court held that the canon was facially unconstitutional, narrowed the canon's language, and remanded the matter for a new finding. The Committee again found the respondent violated the amended canon. Respondent petitioned the court to reject the finding.


OVERVIEW: Upon remand, the commission determined that respondent's communications violated the amended version of Mich. Code Jud. Conduct Canon 7(B)(1)(d), and the committee recommended that the respondent be suspended without pay for 90 days. The commission argued that the respondent, at the time of the campaign, was aware of the obligations imposed by the canon and was accountable for the contents of his campaign literature and that four of the respondent's campaign communications revealed a conscious effort by the respondent to use false statements and to use them with reckless disregard for their truth or falsity as part and parcel of his campaign strategy. The commission concluded that respondent's conduct constituted misconduct in office, clearly prejudicial to the administration of justice. The court disagreed with the commission and concluded that the public communications were not false, and rejected the commission's recommendation of suspension. The respondent did not violate the canon, as the court's review of the exhibits revealed that the communications were either literally true, substantially true despite their inaccuracies, or communicated mere rhetorical hyperbole.


OUTCOME: The court rejected the recommendation of the Judicial Tenure Commission to suspend the respondent from all judicial activities for 90 days.

Tolksdorf v. Griffith


No. 115032


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


464 Mich. 1; 626 N.W.2d 163; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 878


December 12, 2000, Argued  
May 15, 2001, Decided  
May 15, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Updated Copy 6/5/01.


PRIOR HISTORY: Keweenaw Circuit Court, Garfield W. Hood, J. Court of Appeals, WHITBECK, P.J., and MARKMAN and O'CONNELL, JJ. (Docket No. 209240.)
TOLKSDORF v. GRIFFITH, 1999 Mich. App. LEXIS 629 (Mich. Ct. App., June 15, 1999)



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals decision reversed in this case and ruling of trial court for defendants reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought a determination that they had acquired an easement by prescription across defendants' land, and requested a writ of mandamus allowing them to proceed under the Opening of Private Roads and Temporary Highways Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 229.1 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 9.281 et seq.). The Keweenaw Circuit Court (Michigan) denied both requests. The court of appeals reversed as to the private roads act. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were a developer and the owners of lots purchased from that developer. Plaintiff developer's entire parcel was landlocked. Plaintiff sought a road and an easement for utility lines across defendants' neighboring parcel. Defendants refused, resulting in plaintiffs' legal action. The private roads act, dating back to 1850, allowed a private landowner to petition the town supervisor to open a private road across another landowner's property. The court of appeals ordered the writ that would have required the supervisor to initiate a proceeding under the act. The supreme court reversed, holding that the act authorized a taking, as it allowed a permanent physical occupation of private property by means of government action. The taking primarily benefitted only a private rather than a public purpose, and the act required the party petitioning for the road, rather than the state, to compensate the landowner. The court therefore struck down the act as unconstitutional, in violation of both U.S. Const. amend. V and Mich. Const. art 10, § 2.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals decision requiring the town supervisor to initiate a proceeding under the private roads act, and reinstated the trial court ruling. The court struck down the act as unconstitutional.

People v. Sobczak-Obetts


No. 115890


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


463 Mich. 687; 625 N.W.2d 764; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 856


November 14, 2000, Argued  
May 1, 2001, Decided  
May 1, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected September 5, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Kent Circuit Court, Donald A. Johnson, J. Court of Appeals, CAVANAGH, P.J., and HOEKSTRA, J. and GAGE, J. 238 Mich. App. 495 (1999) (Docket No. 214787).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and this matter remanded to trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a possession of a firearm by a felon case, the Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed a trial court's order suppressing the firearms and dismissing the case. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue in this case was whether firearms found in defendant's home, upon execution by federal and state law enforcement officers of a federal search warrant, were properly excluded from evidence in a state prosecution. Defendant argued that Michigan statutory provisions required the officers executing the warrant to provide her with a copy of the affidavit in support of the warrant. Because the officers provided only the warrant itself and not the affidavit, defendant claimed that the search was defective and that the firearms found had to be suppressed. The State argued that, because the only defect in the search and seizure was a technical violation of Michigan's statutory warrant requirements, and because there had been no violation of defendant's constitutional rights under the Fourth Amendment, the exclusionary rule should not have been applied. The instant court held that the lower courts erred in applying the exclusionary rule as a remedy for this statutory violation. The instant court was unable to conclude that the legislature intended the exclusionary rule to apply to a procedural violation of Michigan's statutory warrant requirements at issue in this case.


OUTCOME: Appellate court's judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the instant court's opinion.

Byrne v. State


No. 116412


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


463 Mich. 652; 624 N.W.2d 906; 2001 Mich. LEXIS 600



April 18, 2001, Decided  
April 18, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected September 5, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Kent Circuit Court, Robert A. Benson, J. Court of Appeals, GRIFFIN, P.J., and WILDER and DANHOF, JJ. 239 Mich App 563 (2000) (Docket No. 210852).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, homeowners, challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirming the circuit court's order granting summary disposition in favor of appellees, State, police and contractor.


OVERVIEW: The homeowners challenged the construction of a state police radio tower on a site near their homes because such tower would not comply with restrictions found in the special use permit. As legislative intent was the test for determining whether a governmental unit was immune from the provisions of local zoning ordinances, the supreme court determined that the clear import of the legislature's enactment of 1996 Mich. Pub. Acts 538, which by its terms granted the state police responsibility for all matters concerning construction of the Michigan Public Safety Communications System (MPSCS), was to exempt the state police from local zoning ordinances so that the MPSCS could effectively and efficiently be constructed. Further, the supreme court found that the MPSCS project was not subject to local zoning ordinances or use permits issued under those ordinances, except as specifically provided in Mich. Comp. Laws § 28.282(2) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 4.492(2)). Thus, the court held that the site of the tower was selected in a manner that accorded with the pertinent statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the court of appeals and the circuit court.

Smith v. Cliffs on the Bay Condo. Ass'n.


No. 111587


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


463 Mich. 420; 617 N.W.2d 536; 2000 Mich. LEXIS 2063



October 10, 2000, Decided  
October 10, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 6, 2000. Certiorari Denied May 14, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 3604.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Washtenaw Circuit Court, T. P. Connors, J. Court of Appeals, SAAD, P.J., and O'CONNELL and M. J. MATUZAK, JJ. 226 Mich App 245 (1997) (Docket No. 199498).



DISPOSITION: Remanded case to Court of Appeals for consideration of those issues.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Case returned to court from Court of Appeals (Michigan) after remand for additional findings in quiet title action. Court of appeals ruled defendant association was not given constitutionally adequate notice of tax sale and related redemption periods, reversing circuit court.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs purchased a real estate parcel from the state at a tax sale and received title. Defendant condominium association then claimed ownership. Each party asked the circuit court to quiet title in them. Defendant claimed the procedures by which plaintiffs obtained the title were unconstitutional, particularly attacking the state's notice procedures. The court granted summary disposition for plaintiffs. The court of appeals reversed, ruling defendant was not given constitutionally adequate notice of the tax sale and related redemption periods. The case returned to the court after remand. The court concluded mailing of tax delinquency and redemption notices to a corporation at its tax address of record in the manner required by the General Property Tax Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.1 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.1 et seq.) was sufficient to provide constitutionally adequate notice. It therefore reversed the appellate judgment and reinstated the circuit court's judgment for plaintiffs.


OUTCOME: The appellate judgment was reversed and judgment for plaintiffs was reinstated. Case was remanded to appellate court to consider remaining issues. Appellate court improperly imposed notice requirements beyond those required. Mailing of tax delinquency and redemption notices to corporation at its record tax address in manner required by statute provided constitutionally adequate notice.

 

191 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Crego v. Coleman


No. 113485


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


463 Mich. 248; 615 N.W.2d 218; 2000 Mich. LEXIS 1463


March 8, 2000, Argued  
July 31, 2000, Decided  
July 31, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected September 5, 2001. Certiorari Denied January 8, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 182.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wayne Circuit Court, Marianne O. Battani, J. Court of Appeals, D. E. HOLBROOK JR., P.J., and REILLY, J. and GRIFFIN, J. 201 Mich App 443 (1993) (Crego I) (Docket No. 139068). Court of Appeals, DOCTOROFF, P.J., and MACKENZIE and GRIFFIN, JJ. 226 Mich App 815 (1997) (Crego II) (Docket No. 192798). A special panel, NEFF, SAWYER, MCDONALD, and MURPHY, JJ., FITZGERALD, J., HOEKSTRA, P.J., and WHITBECK, J., 232 Mich App 284 (1998) (Crego III).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a conflict panel of the Court of Appeals, Michigan, holding that a repealed section of a paternity statute was unconstitutional as a violation of the equal protection guarantees of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Mich. Const. art. 1, § 2.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was denied modification of a child support agreement because it was written as unmodifiable pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 722.713 (repealed 1997). Constitutionality of the law was challenged as a violation of the Equal Protection Clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Mich. Const. art. 1, § 2. The statute provided for situations under which illegitimate children received the same right to fully modifiable support as children born during a marriage, so it did not create a classification on the basis of illegitimacy. The statute would be upheld as long as it was rationally related to a permissible legislative objective. It was designed to address paternity proof problems in the support context, which was an important governmental interest. It afforded enhanced opportunities for support of illegitimate children. Because the statute was substantially and directly related to obtaining child support for illegitimate children, and because it was substantially and directly related to the state's legitimate concern regarding proof problems in the child support context, it was not an unconstitutional denial of equal protection under the law.


OUTCOME: The judgment holding a paternity statute provision unconstitutional was reversed because the provision was substantially and directly related to obtaining support for illegitimate children and to the state's legitimate concern regarding proof problems in the child support context. The provision, therefore, did not violate the constitutional guarantees of equal protection.

 

192 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Babick


SC: 115560


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN

193 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Robinson v. City of Detroit


No. 110360, No. 107421


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


462 Mich. 439; 613 N.W.2d 307; 2000 Mich. LEXIS 1427


November 8, 1999, Argued  
July 18, 2000, Decided  
July 11, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] (Coooper) Wayne Circuit Court, Edward M. Thomas, J. Court of Appeals, REILLY, P.J., MICHAEL J. KELLY, and C. L. BOSMAN, JJ. 218 Mich. App. 649 (1996) (Docket No. 175952). (Robinson) Wayne Circuit Court, Susan Bieke Neilson, J. Court of Appeals, O'CONNELL, P.J., and GRIBBS and T. P. PICKARD, JJ. (Docket No. 176421) Court of Appeals, WAHLS, P.J., and HOOD, SAAD, HOEKSTRA, and MARKEY, JJ., JANSEN, and NEFF, JJ. 225 Mich. App. 14 (1997).



DISPOSITION: Result in Robinson affirmed. Cooper is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court consolidated two Courts of Appeals' (Michigan) decisions which were in conflict. One had reversed a trial court's summary disposition for defendants, police officers and city, and the other had affirmed summary disposition for defendants, officers and city, both in plaintiffs', passengers and personal representative, negligence suits for injury and death resulting from the crash of a vehicle which had fled pursuing police.


OVERVIEW: The court consolidated two cases. One case held that police owed passengers in a fleeing car a duty of reasonable care, officers were not immune from liability under the employee provision of the governmental immunity act, and, that because of the motor vehicle exception to governmental immunity, the city was not immune. The other case, a few minutes after the above decision was released, held that police owed no duty to a voluntary passenger in a fleeing car. The court reversed the first and affirmed the second. Where police cars did not hit the fleeing car or physically cause something to hit it, or physically force it off the road or into something, plaintiffs', passengers, injuries did not, as a matter of law, result from the operation of the police cars. Thus the court overruled Fiser v. Ann Arbor, 417 Mich. 461; 339 N.W. 2d 413 (1983), and Rogers v. Detroit, 457 Mich. 125; 579 N.W. 2d 840 (1998). Individual officers were immune from liability where their actions were not "the" proximate cause of the plaintiffs' injuries. Thus the court also overruled Dedes v Asch, 446 Mich. 99; 521 N.W. 2d 488 (1994). Police decision to pursue was also not negligent vehicle operation.


OUTCOME: Reversal of summary disposition by the first appellate court was reversed, affirmation of summary disposition by the second appellate court was affirmed. The court overruled three cases and held that police cars did not, as a matter of law, cause fleeing cars' passengers' injuries where they did not hit, or cause to be hit, the fleeing car; and individual officers were immune from liability if not "the" proximate cause of plaintiffs' injuries.

People v. Bulger


No. 112694


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


462 Mich. 495; 614 N.W.2d 103; 2000 Mich. LEXIS 1437


October 14, 1999, Argued  
July 18, 2000, Decided  
July 18, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied November 13, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 7532.
Writ of certiorari denied Bulger v. Michigan, 531 U.S. 994, 121 S. Ct. 486, 148 L. Ed. 2d 459, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 7532 (2000)
Writ of habeas corpus granted Bulger v. Curtis, 328 F. Supp. 2d 692, 2004 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15277 ( E.D. Mich., 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Saginaw Circuit Court, Leopold P. Borrello, J. Court of Appeals, MARKMAN, P.J., and D. E. HOLBROOK JR., and WHITBECK, JJ. (Docket No. 209031).
People v. Bulger, 1998 Mich. App. LEXIS 2779 (Mich. Ct. App., July 21, 1998)



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals order vacated, the trial court's order denying appointment of counsel reinstated, and remanded to the trial court so that defendant may pursue his application for leave to appeal his convictions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which remanded his case to the trial court for reconsideration of his motion for appointment of appellate counsel.


OVERVIEW: The Michigan Supreme Court granted leave to determine whether an indigent defendant was entitled to the appointment of appellate counsel at public expense when applying for leave to appeal a plea-based conviction. The court vacated the appellate court's decision remanding denial of appointment of appellate counsel to the trial court, holding that neither the state nor the federal constitution required the appointment of counsel under those circumstances. Under the state constitution, the mechanism of appellate review was discretionary. Thus, defendant was not entitled to appointed appellate counsel under either the state or federal constitution. Defendant was not entitled to the appointment of appellate counsel under Mich. Const. art. 1, § 20 because such assistance was not "provided by law" when defendant requested counsel. Moreover, the federal constitution did not mandate appointment of counsel under the present circumstances because defendant had not been denied meaningful access to Michigan's appellate courts.


OUTCOME: Order of the appellate court remanding the case to the trial court for reconsideration was vacated. Trial court's order denying defendant's motion for appointment of appellate counsel was reinstated and remanded to the trial court so that defendant could pursue his application for leave to appeal his convictions.

In reTrejo


No. 112528


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


462 Mich. 341; 612 N.W.2d 407; 2000 Mich. LEXIS 1383


November 10, 1999, Argued  
July 5, 2000, Decided  
July 5, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected July 17, 2000.


PRIOR HISTORY: Oakland County Probate Court, Barry M. Grant, J. Court of Appeals, HOOD, P.J., and MARKMAN and TALBOT, JJ. (Docket No. 200486).
In re TREJO, 1998 Mich. App. LEXIS 1718 (Mich. Ct. App., June 12, 1998)



DISPOSITION: Termination of respondent's parental rights affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent appealed an order of the Oakland County Probate Court (Michigan), which terminated her parental rights pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 712A.19b.


OVERVIEW: The probate court entered an order terminating respondent's parental rights pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 712A.19b. Respondent appealed, contending that Mich. Comp. Laws § 712A.19b(5) violated her due process rights and that the probate court erred in terminating her parental rights. The court affirmed. The court held that § 712A.19b(5) provided that termination of parental rights was mandatory if petitioner established a ground for termination under Mich. Comp. Laws § 712A.19b(3) unless the court found that termination was clearly not in the child's best interest. It was concluded that the probate court did not clearly err in finding that two grounds for termination under § 712A.19b(3) were established, nor did the probate court clearly err in its assessment of the children's best interests. The court also held that § 712A.19b(5) was constitutional.


OUTCOME: Order of the court below terminating respondent's parental rights was affirmed. The court found that the order was not erroneous because there was no error in the conclusion that two statutory grounds for termination were established or in the assessment of the children's best interests.

 

196 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


People v. Toma


No. 112860


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


462 Mich. 281; 613 N.W.2d 694; 2000 Mich. LEXIS 1376


November 9, 1999, Argued  
June 28, 2000, Decided  
June 28, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at PEOPLE v. TOMA, 2000 Mich. App. LEXIS 1076 (Mich. Ct. App., Sept. 22, 2000)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Toma v. Cason, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 53326 ( E.D. Mich., Aug. 2, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Oakland Circuit Court, Barry L. Howard, J. Court of Appeals, NEFF, P.J., and O'CONNELL and YOUNG, JJ. (Docket No. 203029).
PEOPLE v. TOMA, 1998 Mich. App. LEXIS 2057 (Mich. Ct. App., Aug. 4, 1998)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversed and, because defendant raised issues in his cross-appeal not addressed by the Court of Appeals, case remanded to that Court for consideration of the unaddressed issues.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order of the Michigan Court of Appeals, which affirmed the trial court's order granting defendant a new trial on the basis of ineffective counsel, as well as the improper admission of defendant's statements to a forensic psychologist under Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.20a(5) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1043(1)(5)).


OVERVIEW: Following a jury trial, defendant was convicted of first-degree felony murder in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.316(1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.548(1)), and possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony (felony-firearm) in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.227b (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.424(2)). After a hearing on defendant's post-conviction motions, the trial court reversed, agreeing that defendant, whose native language was Arabic and who testified at trial with the aid of an interpreter, had been denied the effective assistance of counsel. The court of appeals affirmed, also finding that the admission of testimony regarding statements made by defendant to a forensic psychologist in contemplation of interposing an insanity defense was error requiring reversal. The court reversed, holding that any error stemming from the introduction of defendant's statements to the forensic psychologist was harmless and that defendant was not denied his constitutional right to the effective assistance of counsel.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; any error stemming from improper admission of defendant's statements to forensic psychologist was harmless, given overwhelming evidence of guilt. Defendant was not denied his right to effective assistance of counsel on the basis of his counsel's failure to ensure that his testimony about what occurred was presented to the jury in a different manner.

 

197 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


Blank v. Department of Corrections


No. 109477


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


462 Mich. 103; 611 N.W.2d 530; 2000 Mich. LEXIS 1193


October 13, 1999, Argued  
June 20, 2000, Decided  
June 20, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Jackson Circuit Court, Charles A. Nelson, J., Ingham Circuit Court, William E. Collette, J. Court of Appeals, MCDONALD, P.J., and MURPHY and M. F. SAPALA, JJ., affirmed. 222 Mich App 385 (1997). (Docket Nos. 188881, 190011).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prison inmates' challenge to the validity of respondent department of corrections' new visitation rules was rejected at the circuit level and the Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirmed, holding unconstitutional 1977 Mich. Pub. Acts 108 §§ 45, 46, which reserved the authority to disapprove executive branch agencies rules.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner prison inmates challenged the validity of visitation rules, which were adopted by respondent department of corrections without approval from the Joint Committee on Administrative Rules. The issue was whether 1977 Mich. Pub. Acts 108 §§ 45, 46 violated the Michigan Constitution by requiring legislative approval of new administrative rules. The action of exercising the authority granted by §§ 45, 46 was subject to enactment and presentment requirements of the Michigan Constitution. Mich. Comp. Laws § 791.206; (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.2276) delegated to respondent the authority to promulgate rules for the management of state penal institutions. 1977 Mich. Pub. Acts 108 was an impermissible expansion of the legislature veto of agency rules by Mich. Const. art. 4, § 37 (1963). Rules were rationally related to respondent's authority, and petitioners did not overcome presumption that enabling act was a constitutional delegation of power to executive branch.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. Legislature's reservation of authority to approve or disapprove rules proposed by executive branch agencies violated separation of powers. Joint Committee on Administrative Rules acted in legislative manner without adhering to enactment and presentment requirements of Michigan Constitution.

200 of 1632 DOCUMENTS


In re Chmura


No. 112062


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


461 Mich. 517; 608 N.W.2d 31; 2000 Mich. LEXIS 567


November 10, 1999, Argued  
March 28, 2000, Decided  
March 28, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 2, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 5206.


PRIOR HISTORY: Judicial Tenure Commission, Wayne Circuit Judge John P. Kirwan.



DISPOSITION: Canon 7(B)(1)(d) is facially unconstitutional. Canon to apply only to public communications that the candidate for judicial office knew were false or were used by the candidate with reckless disregard for their truth or falsity. The determination whether a candidate recklessly disregarded the truth or falsity of a public communication involves an objective inquiry. Remanded to the Judicial Tenure Commission for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, a judge, petitioned the court to reject the Michigan Judicial Tenure Commission's recommendation of suspension for 90 days for misconduct arising out of respondent's judicial campaign literature, which allegedly violated Michigan Code of Judicial Conduct Canon 7(B)(1)(d).


OVERVIEW: Respondent judge challenged the Michigan Judicial Tenure Commission's (JTC) recommendation of his 90-day suspension for disseminating campaign literature that allegedly violated Michigan Code of Judicial Conduct Canon 7(B)(1)(d), arguing the canon violated U.S. Const. amend. I. The court held that Canon 7(B)(1)(d) was facially unconstitutional and narrowed it to prohibit a candidate from knowingly or recklessly using false public communications. The court remanded for a determination whether respondent violated the narrowed canon, and if so, for a new disciplinary recommendation. Canon 7(B)(1)(d) was not narrowly tailored to serve the state's compelling interest in maintaining election process integrity and ensuring public confidence in the judiciary. Canon 7(B)(1)(d) authorized disciplinary action for core political speech by broadly regulating all forms of public communication, creating a realistic danger of a chilling effect. The determination as to respondent's knowledge was to involve an objective inquiry, not an actual malice standard.


OUTCOME: The judicial cannon was facially unconstitutional; the court remanded for a determination in light of the court's narrowing of the cannon of whether respondent's knew his public communications were false or were used with reckless disregard for their truth or falsity, a determination that was to involve an objective inquiry.
